	<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en"><b style="color:#000;background:#ffff66">Self</b>-<b style="color:#000;background:#66ffff">driving</b> car</h1>
	
	<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
		<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
		<div id="contentSub"><span class="mw-redirectedfrom">&#160;&#160;(Redirected from <a href="/w/index.php?title=Autonomous_car&amp;redirect=no" class="mw-redirect" title="Autonomous car">Autonomous car</a>)</span></div>
		
		
		<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
		<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">This article is about the vehicle. For the system, see <a href="/wiki/Automated_driving_system" title="Automated driving system">automated <b style="color:#000;background:#66ffff">driving</b> system</a>. For the wider application of artificial intelligence to automobiles, see <a href="/wiki/Unmanned_ground_vehicle" title="Unmanned ground vehicle">Unmanned ground vehicle</a>.</div>
<table class="box-Cleanup_reorganize plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-style" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><img alt="Ambox rewrite.svg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Ambox_rewrite.svg/40px-Ambox_rewrite.svg.png" decoding="async" width="40" height="40" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Ambox_rewrite.svg/60px-Ambox_rewrite.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Ambox_rewrite.svg/80px-Ambox_rewrite.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="620" data-file-height="620" /></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This article <b>may be in need of reorganization to comply with Wikipedia's <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Layout" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Layout">layout guidelines</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help by <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Self-driving_car&amp;action=edit">editing the article</a> to make improvements to the overall structure.</span>  <small class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">March 2018</span>)</i></small><small class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-move" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Split-arrows.svg/50px-Split-arrows.svg.png" decoding="async" width="50" height="17" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Split-arrows.svg/75px-Split-arrows.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Split-arrows.svg/100px-Split-arrows.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="60" data-file-height="20" /></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">It has been suggested that portions of this section be <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Splitting" title="Wikipedia:Splitting">split</a> out  into another article&#32;titled <i><a href="/wiki/Vehicular_automation" title="Vehicular automation">Vehicular automation</a></i>. (<a href="/wiki/Talk:Self-driving_car" title="Talk:Self-driving car">Discuss</a>) <small><i>(May 2020)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Waymo_Chrysler_Pacifica_in_Los_Altos,_2017.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Waymo_Chrysler_Pacifica_in_Los_Altos%2C_2017.jpg/220px-Waymo_Chrysler_Pacifica_in_Los_Altos%2C_2017.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="119" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Waymo_Chrysler_Pacifica_in_Los_Altos%2C_2017.jpg/330px-Waymo_Chrysler_Pacifica_in_Los_Altos%2C_2017.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Waymo_Chrysler_Pacifica_in_Los_Altos%2C_2017.jpg/440px-Waymo_Chrysler_Pacifica_in_Los_Altos%2C_2017.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5015" data-file-height="2711" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Waymo_Chrysler_Pacifica_in_Los_Altos,_2017.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Waymo" title="Waymo">Waymo</a> <a href="/wiki/Chrysler_Pacifica_Hybrid" class="mw-redirect" title="Chrysler Pacifica Hybrid">Chrysler Pacifica Hybrid</a> undergoing testing in the San Francisco Bay Area</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Roborace_NYC_ePrix.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Roborace_NYC_ePrix.jpg/220px-Roborace_NYC_ePrix.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="124" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Roborace_NYC_ePrix.jpg/330px-Roborace_NYC_ePrix.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Roborace_NYC_ePrix.jpg/440px-Roborace_NYC_ePrix.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1341" data-file-height="754" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Roborace_NYC_ePrix.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Autonomous racing car on display at the <a href="/wiki/2017_New_York_City_ePrix" title="2017 New York City ePrix">2017 New York City ePrix</a></div></div></div>
<p>A <b><b style="color:#000;background:#ffff66">self</b>-<b style="color:#000;background:#66ffff">driving</b> car</b>, also known as an <b>autonomous vehicle</b> (<b>AV</b>), <b>connected and autonomous vehicle</b> (<b>CAV</b>), <b>driverless car</b>, <b>robo-car</b>, or <b>robotic car</b>,<sup id="cite_ref-:5_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:5-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-thrun2010toward_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-thrun2010toward-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> is a <a href="/wiki/Vehicular_automation" title="Vehicular automation">vehicle</a> that is capable of sensing its environment and moving safely with little or no <a href="/wiki/User_interface" title="User interface">human input</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-:5_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:5-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><b style="color:#000;background:#ffff66">Self</b>-<b style="color:#000;background:#66ffff">driving</b> <b style="color:#000;background:#ffcc99">cars</b> combine a variety of sensors to perceive their surroundings, such as <a href="/wiki/Radar" title="Radar">radar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lidar" title="Lidar">lidar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sonar" title="Sonar">sonar</a>, <a href="/wiki/GPS" class="mw-redirect" title="GPS">GPS</a>, <a href="/wiki/Odometry" title="Odometry">odometry</a> and <a href="/wiki/Inertial_measurement_unit" title="Inertial measurement unit">inertial measurement units</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-:5_1-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:5-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> Advanced <a href="/wiki/Control_system" title="Control system">control systems</a> interpret <a href="/wiki/Sensory_information" class="mw-redirect" title="Sensory information">sensory information</a> to identify appropriate navigation paths, as well as obstacles and relevant <a href="/wiki/Road_signs" class="mw-redirect" title="Road signs">signage</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:7_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:7-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Long-distance <a href="/wiki/Road_transport#Trucking_and_haulage" title="Road transport">trucking</a> is seen as being at the forefront of adopting and implementing the technology.<sup id="cite_ref-Trucks_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Trucks-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r886046785">.mw-parser-output .toclimit-2 .toclevel-1 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-3 .toclevel-2 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-4 .toclevel-3 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-5 .toclevel-4 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-6 .toclevel-5 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-7 .toclevel-6 ul{display:none}</style><div class="toclimit-3"><div id="toc" class="toc" role="navigation" aria-labelledby="mw-toc-heading"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" style="display:none" /><div class="toctitle" lang="en" dir="ltr"><h2 id="mw-toc-heading">Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Self-driving_car&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_self-driving_cars" title="History of self-driving cars">History of <b style="color:#000;background:#ffff66">self</b>-<b style="color:#000;background:#66ffff">driving</b> <b style="color:#000;background:#ffcc99">cars</b></a></div>
<p>Experiments have been conducted on automated <b style="color:#000;background:#66ffff">driving</b> systems (ADS) since at least the 1920s;<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> trials began in the 1950s. The first semi-automated car was developed in 1977, by Japan's Tsukuba Mechanical Engineering Laboratory, which required specially marked streets that were interpreted by two cameras on the vehicle and an analog computer. The vehicle reached speeds up to 30 kilometres per hour (19&#160;mph) with the support of an elevated rail.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A landmark autonomous car appeared in the 1980s, with <a href="/wiki/Carnegie_Mellon_University" title="Carnegie Mellon University">Carnegie Mellon University</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Navlab" title="Navlab">Navlab</a><sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> and ALV<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> projects funded by the United States' <a href="/wiki/Defense_Advanced_Research_Projects_Agency" class="mw-redirect" title="Defense Advanced Research Projects Agency">Defense Advanced Research Projects Agency</a> (DARPA) starting in 1984 and <a href="/wiki/Mercedes-Benz" title="Mercedes-Benz">Mercedes-Benz</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bundeswehr_University_Munich" title="Bundeswehr University Munich">Bundeswehr University Munich</a>'s <a href="/wiki/EUREKA_Prometheus_Project" class="mw-redirect" title="EUREKA Prometheus Project">EUREKA Prometheus Project</a> in 1987.<sup id="cite_ref-idsia_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-idsia-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> By 1985, the ALV had demonstrated <b style="color:#000;background:#ffff66">self</b>-<b style="color:#000;background:#66ffff">driving</b> speeds on two-lane roads of 31 kilometres per hour (19&#160;mph), with obstacle avoidance added in 1986, and off-road <b style="color:#000;background:#66ffff">driving</b> in day and nighttime conditions by 1987.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> A major milestone was achieved in 1995, with <a href="/wiki/Carnegie_Mellon_University" title="Carnegie Mellon University">CMU's</a> <a href="/wiki/Navlab" title="Navlab">NavLab 5</a> completing the first autonomous coast-to-coast drive of the United States. Of the 2,849&#160;mi (4,585&#160;km) between <a href="/wiki/Pittsburgh,_Pennsylvania" class="mw-redirect" title="Pittsburgh, Pennsylvania">Pittsburgh, Pennsylvania</a> and <a href="/wiki/San_Diego,_California" class="mw-redirect" title="San Diego, California">San Diego, California</a>, 2,797&#160;mi (4,501&#160;km) were autonomous (98.2%), completed with an average speed of 63.8&#160;mph (102.7&#160;km/h).<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> From the 1960s through the second <a href="/wiki/DARPA_Grand_Challenge_(2005)" title="DARPA Grand Challenge (2005)">DARPA Grand Challenge</a> in 2005, automated vehicle research in the United States was primarily funded by DARPA, the US Army, and the US Navy, yielding incremental advances in speeds, <b style="color:#000;background:#66ffff">driving</b> competence in more complex conditions, controls, and sensor systems.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> Companies and research organizations have developed prototypes.<sup id="cite_ref-idsia_15-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-idsia-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BoschAutomatedDriving_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BoschAutomatedDriving-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-CarMakerOne_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CarMakerOne-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-CarMakerTwo_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CarMakerTwo-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ToyotaBot_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ToyotaBot-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-GoogleOnWheels_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GoogleOnWheels-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Vislab_electric_autonomous_car_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vislab_electric_autonomous_car-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The US allocated <a href="/wiki/US$" class="mw-redirect" title="US$">US$</a>650 million in 1991 for research on the National Automated Highway System, which demonstrated automated <b style="color:#000;background:#66ffff">driving</b> through a combination of automation embedded in the highway with automated technology in vehicles, and cooperative networking between the vehicles and with the highway infrastructure. The program concluded with a successful demonstration in 1997 but without clear direction or funding to implement the system on a larger scale.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> Partly funded by the National Automated Highway System and DARPA, the Carnegie Mellon University Navlab drove 4,584 kilometres (2,848&#160;mi) across America in 1995, 4,501 kilometres (2,797&#160;mi) or 98% of it autonomously.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> Navlab's record achievement stood unmatched for two decades until 2015, when Delphi improved it by piloting an Audi, augmented with Delphi technology, over 5,472 kilometres (3,400&#160;mi) through 15 states while remaining in <b style="color:#000;background:#ffff66">self</b>-<b style="color:#000;background:#66ffff">driving</b> mode 99% of the time.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> In 2015, the US states of <a href="/wiki/Nevada" title="Nevada">Nevada</a>, <a href="/wiki/Florida" title="Florida">Florida</a>, <a href="/wiki/California" title="California">California</a>, <a href="/wiki/Virginia" title="Virginia">Virginia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Michigan" title="Michigan">Michigan</a>, together with <a href="/wiki/Washington,_DC" class="mw-redirect" title="Washington, DC">Washington, DC</a>, allowed the testing of automated <b style="color:#000;background:#ffcc99">cars</b> on public roads.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>From 2016 to 2018, the <a href="/wiki/European_Commission" title="European Commission">European Commission</a> funded an innovation strategy development for connected and automated <b style="color:#000;background:#66ffff">driving</b> through the Coordination Actions CARTRE and SCOUT.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> Moreover, the Strategic Transport Research and Innovation Agenda (STRIA) Roadmap for Connected and Automated Transport was published in 2019.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2017, <a href="/wiki/Audi" title="Audi">Audi</a> stated that its latest <a href="/wiki/Audi_A8" title="Audi A8">A8</a> would be automated at speeds of up to 60 kilometres per hour (37&#160;mph) using its "Audi AI". The driver would not have to do safety checks such as frequently gripping the steering wheel. The Audi A8 was claimed to be the first production car to reach Level 3 automated <b style="color:#000;background:#66ffff">driving</b>, and Audi would be the first manufacturer to use laser scanners in addition to cameras and ultrasonic sensors for their system.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In November 2017, <a href="/wiki/Waymo" title="Waymo">Waymo</a> announced that it had begun testing driverless <b style="color:#000;background:#ffcc99">cars</b> without a safety driver in the driver position;<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> however, there was still an employee in the car.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> In October 2018, Waymo announced that its test vehicles had traveled in automated mode for over 10,000,000 miles (16,000,000&#160;km), increasing by about 1,000,000 miles (1,600,000 kilometres) per month.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> In December 2018, Waymo was the first to commercialize a fully autonomous taxi service in the US, in Phoenix, Arizona.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A*STAR's Institute for Infocomm Research (I<sup>2</sup>R) developed a <b style="color:#000;background:#ffff66">self</b>-<b style="color:#000;background:#66ffff">driving</b> vehicle which was the first to be approved in Singapore for public road testing at <a href="/wiki/One-north" title="One-north">one-north</a> in July 2015. It has ferried several dignitaries such as Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Lee_Hsien_Loong" title="Lee Hsien Loong">Lee Hsien Loong</a>, Minister <a href="/wiki/S._Iswaran" title="S. Iswaran">S. Iswaran</a>, Minister <a href="/wiki/Vivian_Balakrishnan" title="Vivian Balakrishnan">Vivian Balakrishnan</a>, and several ministers from other countries.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2020, a <a href="/wiki/National_Transportation_Safety_Board" title="National Transportation Safety Board">National Transportation Safety Board</a> chairman clarified there is no <b style="color:#000;background:#ffff66">self</b>-<b style="color:#000;background:#66ffff">driving</b> car in the US in 2020:
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r960796168">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>There is not a vehicle currently available to US consumers that is <b style="color:#000;background:#ffff66">self</b>-<b style="color:#000;background:#66ffff">driving</b>. Period. Every vehicle sold to US consumers still requires the driver to be actively engaged in the <b style="color:#000;background:#66ffff">driving</b> task, even when advanced driver assistance systems are activated. If you are selling a car with an advanced driver assistance system, you’re not selling a <b style="color:#000;background:#ffff66">self</b>-<b style="color:#000;background:#66ffff">driving</b> car. If you are <b style="color:#000;background:#66ffff">driving</b> a car with an advanced driver assistance system, you don’t own a <b style="color:#000;background:#ffff66">self</b>-<b style="color:#000;background:#66ffff">driving</b> car<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Definitions">Definitions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Self-driving_car&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Definitions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>There is some inconsistency in the terminology used in the <b style="color:#000;background:#ffff66">self</b>-<b style="color:#000;background:#66ffff">driving</b> car industry. Various organizations have proposed to define an accurate and consistent vocabulary.
</p><p>Such confusion has been documented in <a href="/wiki/Self-driving_car#Classification" title="Self-driving car">SAE J3016</a> which states that "Some vernacular usages associate autonomous specifically with full <b style="color:#000;background:#66ffff">driving</b> automation (Level 5), while other usages apply it to all levels of <b style="color:#000;background:#66ffff">driving</b> automation, and some state legislation has defined it to correspond approximately to any ADS [automated <b style="color:#000;background:#66ffff">driving</b> system] at or above Level 3 (or to any vehicle equipped with such an ADS)."
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Terminology_and_safety_considerations">Terminology and safety considerations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Self-driving_car&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Terminology and safety considerations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Modern vehicles provide partly automated features such as keeping the car within its lane, speed controls or emergency braking. Nonetheless, differences remain between a fully autonomous <b style="color:#000;background:#ffff66">self</b>-<b style="color:#000;background:#66ffff">driving</b> car on one hand and <a href="/wiki/Advanced_driver-assistance_systems" title="Advanced driver-assistance systems">driver assistance technologies</a> on the other hand. According to the <a href="/wiki/BBC" title="BBC">BBC</a>, confusion between those concepts leads to deaths.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Association_of_British_Insurers" title="Association of British Insurers">Association of British Insurers</a> considers the usage of the word <i>autonomous</i> in marketing for modern <b style="color:#000;background:#ffcc99">cars</b> to be dangerous because car ads make motorists think 'autonomous' and 'autopilot' means a vehicle can drive itself when they still rely on the driver to ensure safety. Technology alone still is not able to drive the car.
</p><p>When some car makers suggest or claim vehicles are <i><b style="color:#000;background:#ffff66">self</b>-<b style="color:#000;background:#66ffff">driving</b></i>, when they are only partly automated, drivers risk becoming excessively confident, leading to crashes, while fully <b style="color:#000;background:#ffff66">self</b>-<b style="color:#000;background:#66ffff">driving</b> <b style="color:#000;background:#ffcc99">cars</b> are still a long way off in the UK.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Autonomous_vs._automated">Autonomous vs. automated</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Self-driving_car&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Autonomous vs. automated">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><i>Autonomous</i> means <b style="color:#000;background:#ffff66">self</b>-governing.<sup id="cite_ref-antsaklis1991introduction_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-antsaklis1991introduction-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> Many historical projects related to vehicle automation have been <i>automated</i> (made automatic) subject to a heavy reliance on artificial aids in their environment, such as magnetic strips. Autonomous control implies satisfactory performance under significant uncertainties in the environment and the ability to compensate for system failures without external intervention.<sup id="cite_ref-antsaklis1991introduction_46-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-antsaklis1991introduction-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>One approach is to implement <a href="/wiki/Communication_networks" class="mw-redirect" title="Communication networks">communication networks</a> both in the immediate vicinity (for <a href="/wiki/Collision_avoidance_system" title="Collision avoidance system">collision avoidance</a>) and farther away (for congestion management). Such outside influences in the decision process reduce an individual vehicle's autonomy, while still not requiring human intervention.
</p><p>Wood et al. (2012) wrote, "This Article generally uses the term 'autonomous,' instead of the term 'automated.' " The term "autonomous" was chosen "because it is the term that is currently in more widespread use (and thus is more familiar to the general public). However, the latter term is arguably more accurate. 'Automated' connotes control or operation by a machine, while 'autonomous' connotes acting alone or independently. Most of the vehicle concepts (that we are currently aware of) have a person in the driver's seat, utilize a communication connection to the Cloud or other vehicles, and do not independently select either destinations or routes for reaching them. Thus, the term 'automated' would more accurately describe these vehicle concepts."<sup id="cite_ref-Woodetal_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Woodetal-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> As of 2017, most commercial projects focused on automated vehicles that did not communicate with other vehicles or with an enveloping management regime.
EuroNCAP defines autonomous in "Autonomous Emergency Braking" as: "the system acts independently of the driver to avoid or mitigate the accident." which implies the autonomous system is not the driver.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Nonetheless, the words <i>automated</i> and <i>autonomous</i> might also be used together. For instance, Regulation (EU) 2019/2144 of the European Parliament and of the Council of 27 November 2019 on type-approval requirements for motor vehicles (...) defines "automated vehicle" and "fully automated vehicle" based on their autonomous capacity:<sup id="cite_ref-Regulation_eU_2019_2144_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Regulation_eU_2019_2144-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>"automated vehicle" means a motor vehicle designed and constructed to move autonomously for certain periods of time without continuous driver supervision but in respect of which driver intervention is still expected or required;<sup id="cite_ref-Regulation_eU_2019_2144_49-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Regulation_eU_2019_2144-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>"fully automated vehicle" means a motor vehicle that has been designed and constructed to move autonomously without any driver supervision;<sup id="cite_ref-Regulation_eU_2019_2144_49-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Regulation_eU_2019_2144-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Autonomous_versus_cooperative">Autonomous versus cooperative</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Self-driving_car&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Autonomous versus cooperative">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>To enable a car to travel without any driver embedded within the vehicle, some companies use a remote driver.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>
According to <a href="/wiki/Self-driving_car#Classification" title="Self-driving car">SAE J3016</a>, <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r960796168"/></p><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Some <b style="color:#000;background:#66ffff">driving</b> automation systems may indeed be autonomous if they perform all of their functions independently and <b style="color:#000;background:#ffff66">self</b>-sufficiently, but if they depend on communication and/or cooperation with outside entities, they should be considered cooperative rather than autonomous.</p></blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Self-driving_car"><b style="color:#000;background:#ffff66">Self</b>-<b style="color:#000;background:#66ffff">driving</b> car</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Self-driving_car&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Self-driving car">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><i><a href="/wiki/PC_Magazine" title="PC Magazine">PC Magazine</a></i> defines a <b style="color:#000;background:#ffff66">self</b>-<b style="color:#000;background:#66ffff">driving</b> car as "A computer-controlled car that drives itself."<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Union_of_Concerned_Scientists" title="Union of Concerned Scientists">Union of Concerned Scientists</a> states that <b style="color:#000;background:#ffff66">self</b>-<b style="color:#000;background:#66ffff">driving</b> <b style="color:#000;background:#ffcc99">cars</b> are "<b style="color:#000;background:#ffcc99">cars</b> or trucks in which human drivers are never required to take control to safely operate the vehicle. Also known as autonomous or 'driverless' <b style="color:#000;background:#ffcc99">cars</b>, they combine sensors and software to control, navigate, and drive the vehicle."<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Classification">Classification</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Self-driving_car&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Classification">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Tesla_Autopilot_Engaged_in_Model_X.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Tesla_Autopilot_Engaged_in_Model_X.jpg/220px-Tesla_Autopilot_Engaged_in_Model_X.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Tesla_Autopilot_Engaged_in_Model_X.jpg/330px-Tesla_Autopilot_Engaged_in_Model_X.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Tesla_Autopilot_Engaged_in_Model_X.jpg/440px-Tesla_Autopilot_Engaged_in_Model_X.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4032" data-file-height="3024" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Tesla_Autopilot_Engaged_in_Model_X.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Tesla Autopilot system is classified as an SAE Level 2 system<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>A classification system with six levels – ranging from fully manual to fully automated systems – was published in 2014 by <a href="/wiki/SAE_International" title="SAE International">SAE International</a>, an automotive standardization body, as J3016, <i>Taxonomy and Definitions for Terms Related to On-Road Motor Vehicle Automated <b style="color:#000;background:#66ffff">Driving</b> Systems</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> This classification is based on the amount of driver intervention and attentiveness required, rather than the vehicle's capabilities, although these are loosely related. In the United States in 2013, the <a href="/wiki/National_Highway_Traffic_Safety_Administration" title="National Highway Traffic Safety Administration">National Highway Traffic Safety Administration</a> (NHTSA) released a formal classification system,<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> but abandoned it in favor of the SAE standard in 2016. Also in 2016, SAE updated its classification, called J3016_201609.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><span id="automation-levels"></span>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Levels_of_driving_automation">Levels of <b style="color:#000;background:#66ffff">driving</b> automation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Self-driving_car&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Levels of driving automation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In SAE's automation level definitions, "<b style="color:#000;background:#66ffff">driving</b> mode" means "a type of <b style="color:#000;background:#66ffff">driving</b> scenario with characteristic dynamic <b style="color:#000;background:#66ffff">driving</b> task requirements (e.g., expressway merging, high speed cruising, low speed traffic jam, closed-campus operations, etc.)"<sup id="cite_ref-:5_1-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:5-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-SAE_definitions_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SAE_definitions-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>Level 0: The automated system issues warnings and may momentarily intervene but has no sustained vehicle control.</li>
<li>Level 1 ("hands on"): The driver and the automated system share control of the vehicle. Examples are systems where the driver controls steering and the automated system controls engine power to maintain a set speed (<a href="/wiki/Cruise_control" title="Cruise control">Cruise Control</a>) or engine and brake power to maintain and vary speed (<a href="/wiki/Adaptive_Cruise_Control" class="mw-redirect" title="Adaptive Cruise Control">Adaptive Cruise Control</a> or ACC); and <a href="/wiki/Automatic_Parking" class="mw-redirect" title="Automatic Parking">Parking Assistance</a>, where steering is automated while speed is under manual control. The driver must be ready to retake full control at any time. <a href="/wiki/Lane_departure_warning_system" title="Lane departure warning system">Lane Keeping Assistance</a> (LKA) Type II is a further example of Level 1 <b style="color:#000;background:#ffff66">self</b>-<b style="color:#000;background:#66ffff">driving</b>. A <a href="/wiki/Automatic_Emergency_Braking" class="mw-redirect" title="Automatic Emergency Braking">collision mitigation system</a> which alerts the driver to a crash and permits full braking capacity is also a Level 1 feature, according to Autopilot Review magazine.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>Level 2 ("hands off"): The automated system takes full control of the vehicle: accelerating, braking, and <a href="/wiki/Lane_centering" title="Lane centering">steering</a>. The driver must monitor the <b style="color:#000;background:#66ffff">driving</b> and be prepared to intervene immediately at any time if the automated system fails to respond properly. The shorthand "hands off" is not meant to be taken literally – contact between hand and wheel is often mandatory during SAE 2 <b style="color:#000;background:#66ffff">driving</b>, to confirm that the driver is ready to intervene. The eyes of the driver might be monitored by cameras to confirm that the driver is keeping his/her attention to traffic.</li>
<li>Level 3 ("eyes off"): The driver can safely turn their attention away from the <b style="color:#000;background:#66ffff">driving</b> tasks, e.g. the driver can text or watch a movie. The vehicle will handle situations that call for an immediate response, like emergency braking. The driver must still be prepared to intervene within some limited time, specified by the manufacturer, when called upon by the vehicle to do so. You can think of the automated system as a co-driver that will alert you in an orderly fashion when it is your turn to drive. An example would be a Traffic Jam Chauffeur.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>Level 4 ("mind off"): As level 3, but no driver attention is ever required for safety, e.g. the driver may safely go to sleep or leave the driver's seat. <b style="color:#000;background:#ffff66">Self</b>-<b style="color:#000;background:#66ffff">driving</b> is supported only in limited spatial areas (<a href="/wiki/Geo-fence" title="Geo-fence">geofenced</a>) or under special circumstances. Outside of these areas or circumstances, the vehicle must be able to safely abort the trip, e.g. park the car, if the driver does not retake control. An example would be a robotic taxi or a robotic delivery service that only covers selected locations in a specific area.</li>
<li>Level 5 ("steering wheel optional"): No human intervention is required at all. An example would be a robotic taxi that works on all roads all over the world, all year around, in all weather conditions.</li></ul>
<p>In the formal SAE definition below, note in particular the shift from SAE 2 to SAE 3: the human driver no longer has to monitor the environment. This is the final aspect of the "dynamic <b style="color:#000;background:#66ffff">driving</b> task" that is now passed over from the human to the automated system. At SAE 3, the human driver still has responsibility to intervene when asked to do so by the automated system. At SAE 4 the human driver is always relieved of that responsibility and at SAE 5 the automated system will never need to ask for an intervention.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legal_definition">Legal definition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Self-driving_car&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Legal definition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2><p>
In <a href="/wiki/Washington,_DC" class="mw-redirect" title="Washington, DC">Washington, DC</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Code_of_the_District_of_Columbia" title="Code of the District of Columbia">district code</a>: <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r960796168"/></p><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>"Autonomous vehicle" means a vehicle capable of navigating District roadways and interpreting traffic-control devices without a driver actively operating any of the vehicle's control systems. The term "autonomous vehicle" excludes a motor vehicle enabled with active safety systems or driver- assistance systems, including systems to provide electronic blind-spot assistance, crash avoidance, emergency braking, parking assistance, adaptive cruise control, lane-keep assistance, lane-departure warning, or traffic-jam and queuing assistance, unless the system alone or in combination with other systems enables the vehicle on which the technology is installed to drive without active control or monitoring by a human operator.</p></blockquote>
<p>In the same district code, it is considered that:
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r960796168"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>An autonomous vehicle may operate on a public roadway; provided, that the vehicle:
</p><ul><li>(1) Has a manual override feature that allows a driver to assume control of the autonomous vehicle at any time;</li>
<li>(2) Has a driver seated in the control seat of the vehicle while in operation who is prepared to take control of the autonomous vehicle at any moment; and</li>
<li>(3) Is capable of operating in compliance with the District's applicable traffic laws and motor vehicle laws and traffic control devices.</li></ul></blockquote>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Semi-automated_vehicles">Semi-automated vehicles</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Self-driving_car&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Semi-automated vehicles">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Between manually driven vehicles (SAE Level 0) and fully autonomous vehicles (SAE Level 5), there are a variety of vehicle types that can be described to have some degree of <a href="/wiki/Vehicular_automation" title="Vehicular automation">automation</a>. These are collectively known as semi-automated vehicles. As it could be a while before the technology and infrastructure are developed for full automation, it is likely that vehicles will have increasing levels of automation. These semi-automated vehicles could potentially harness many of the advantages of fully automated vehicles, while still keeping the driver in charge of the vehicle.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Technical_challenges">Technical challenges</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Self-driving_car&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Technical challenges">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Hybrid_navigation" title="Hybrid navigation">Hybrid navigation</a></div>
<p>There are different systems that help the <b style="color:#000;background:#ffff66">self</b>-<b style="color:#000;background:#66ffff">driving</b> car control the car. Systems that need improvement include the car navigation system, the location system, the electronic map, the map matching, the global path planning, the environment perception, the laser perception, the radar perception, the visual perception, the vehicle control, the perception of vehicle speed and direction, and the vehicle control method.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The challenge for driverless car designers is to produce control systems capable of analyzing sensory data in order to provide accurate detection of other vehicles and the road ahead.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> Modern <b style="color:#000;background:#ffff66">self</b>-<b style="color:#000;background:#66ffff">driving</b> <b style="color:#000;background:#ffcc99">cars</b> generally use <a href="/wiki/Bayes%27_theorem" title="Bayes&#39; theorem">Bayesian</a> <a href="/wiki/Simultaneous_localization_and_mapping" title="Simultaneous localization and mapping">simultaneous localization and mapping</a> (SLAM) algorithms,<sup id="cite_ref-IEEE1_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IEEE1-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> which fuse data from multiple sensors and an off-line map into current location estimates and map updates. Waymo has developed a variant of SLAM with detection and tracking of other moving objects (DATMO), which also handles obstacles such as <b style="color:#000;background:#ffcc99">cars</b> and pedestrians. Simpler systems may use roadside <a href="/wiki/Real-time_locating_system" title="Real-time locating system">real-time locating system</a> (RTLS) technologies to aid localization. Typical sensors include <a href="/wiki/Lidar" title="Lidar">lidar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Stereo_vision" class="mw-redirect" title="Stereo vision">stereo vision</a>, <a href="/wiki/GPS" class="mw-redirect" title="GPS">GPS</a> and <a href="/wiki/Inertial_measurement_unit" title="Inertial measurement unit">IMU</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-arxiv_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-arxiv-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-IEEE2_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IEEE2-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> Control systems on automated <b style="color:#000;background:#ffcc99">cars</b> may use <a href="/wiki/Sensor_Fusion" class="mw-redirect" title="Sensor Fusion">Sensor Fusion</a>, which is an approach that integrates information from a variety of sensors on the car to produce a more consistent, accurate, and useful view of the environment.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> Heavy rainfall, hail, or snow could impede the car sensors.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>Driverless vehicles require some form of <a href="/wiki/Machine_vision" title="Machine vision">machine vision</a> for the purpose of visual object recognition. Automated <b style="color:#000;background:#ffcc99">cars</b> are being developed with <a href="/wiki/Deep_neural_networks" class="mw-redirect" title="Deep neural networks">deep neural networks</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-arxiv_64-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-arxiv-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> a type of <a href="/wiki/Deep_learning" title="Deep learning">deep learning</a> architecture with many computational stages, or levels, in which neurons are simulated from the environment that activate the network.<sup id="cite_ref-science_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-science-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> The neural network depends on an extensive amount of data extracted from real-life <b style="color:#000;background:#66ffff">driving</b> scenarios,<sup id="cite_ref-arxiv_64-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-arxiv-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> enabling the neural network to "learn" how to execute the best course of action.<sup id="cite_ref-science_67-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-science-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In May 2018, researchers from the <a href="/wiki/Massachusetts_Institute_of_Technology" title="Massachusetts Institute of Technology">Massachusetts Institute of Technology</a> announced that they had built an automated car that can navigate unmapped roads.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> Researchers at their <a href="/wiki/MIT_Computer_Science_and_Artificial_Intelligence_Laboratory" title="MIT Computer Science and Artificial Intelligence Laboratory">Computer Science and Artificial Intelligence Laboratory</a> (CSAIL) have developed a new system, called MapLite, which allows <b style="color:#000;background:#ffff66">self</b>-<b style="color:#000;background:#66ffff">driving</b> <b style="color:#000;background:#ffcc99">cars</b> to drive on roads that they have never been on before, without using 3D maps. The system combines the GPS position of the vehicle, a "sparse topological map" such as <a href="/wiki/OpenStreetMap" title="OpenStreetMap">OpenStreetMap</a>, (i.e. having 2D features of the roads only), and a series of sensors that observe the road conditions.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Nature_of_the_digital_technology">Nature of the digital technology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Self-driving_car&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Nature of the digital technology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<table class="box-Essay-like plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-style ambox-essay-like" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f2/Edit-clear.svg/40px-Edit-clear.svg.png" decoding="async" width="40" height="40" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f2/Edit-clear.svg/60px-Edit-clear.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f2/Edit-clear.svg/80px-Edit-clear.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="48" data-file-height="48" /></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>is written like a <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:What_Wikipedia_is_not#Wikipedia_is_not_a_publisher_of_original_thought" title="Wikipedia:What Wikipedia is not">personal reflection, personal essay, or argumentative essay</a></b> that states a Wikipedia editor's personal feelings or presents an original argument about a topic.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Self-driving_car&amp;action=edit">help improve it</a> by rewriting it in an <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Writing_better_articles#Information_style_and_tone" title="Wikipedia:Writing better articles">encyclopedic style</a>.</span>  <small class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">November 2019</span>)</i></small><small class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Autonomous vehicles, as digital technology, have certain characteristics that distinguish them from other types of technologies and vehicles. Due to these characteristics, autonomous vehicles are able to be more transformative and agile to possible changes. The characteristics will be explained based on the following subjects: homogenization and decoupling, connectivity, reprogrammable and smart, digital traces and modularity.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Homogenization_and_decoupling">Homogenization and decoupling</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Self-driving_car&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Homogenization and decoupling">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Homogenization comes from the fact that all digital information assumes the same form. During the ongoing evolution of the digital era, certain industry standards have been developed on how to store digital information and in what type of format. This concept of homogenization also applies to autonomous vehicles. In order for autonomous vehicles to perceive their surroundings, they have to use different techniques each with their own accompanying digital information (e.g. radar, GPS, motion sensors and computer vision). Due to homogenization, the digital information from these different techniques is stored in a homogeneous way. This implies that all digital information comes in the same form, which means their differences are decoupled, and digital information can be transmitted, stored and computed in a way that the vehicles and its operating system can better understand and act upon it. Homogenization also helps to exponentially increase the computing power of hard- and software (Moore's law) which also supports the autonomous vehicles to understand and act upon the digital information in a more cost-effective way, therefore lowering the marginal costs.;
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Connectivity">Connectivity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Self-driving_car&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Connectivity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Connectivity means that users of a certain digital technology can connect easily with other users, other applications or even other enterprises. In the case of autonomous vehicles, it is essential for them to connect with other 'devices' in order to function most effectively. Autonomous vehicles are equipped with communication systems which allow them to communicate with other autonomous vehicles and roadside units to provide them, amongst other things, with information about road work or traffic congestion. In addition, scientists believe that the future will have computer programs that connect and manage each individual autonomous vehicle as it navigates through an intersection. This type of connectivity must replace traffic lights and stop signs.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> These types of characteristics drive and further develop the ability of autonomous vehicles to understand and cooperate with other products and services (such as intersection computer systems) in the autonomous vehicles market. This could lead to a network of autonomous vehicles all using the same network and information available on that network. Eventually, this can lead to more autonomous vehicles using the network because the information has been validated through the usage of other autonomous vehicles. Such movements will strengthen the value of the network and is called network externalities.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reprogrammable">Reprogrammable</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Self-driving_car&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Reprogrammable">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Another characteristic of autonomous vehicles is that the core product will have a greater emphasis on the software and its possibilities, instead of the chassis and its engine. This is because autonomous vehicles have software systems that drive the vehicle meaning that updates through reprogramming or editing the software can enhance the benefits of the owner (e.g. update in better distinguishing blind person vs. non-blind person so that the vehicle will take extra caution when approaching a blind person). A characteristic of this reprogrammable part of autonomous vehicles is that the updates need not only to come from the supplier, because through machine learning, smart autonomous vehicles can generate certain updates and install them accordingly (e.g. new navigation maps or new intersection computer systems). These reprogrammable characteristics of the digital technology and the possibility of smart machine learning give manufacturers of autonomous vehicles the opportunity to differentiate themselves on software. This also implies that autonomous vehicles are never finished because the product can continuously be improved.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Digital_traces">Digital traces</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Self-driving_car&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Digital traces">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Autonomous vehicles are equipped with different sorts of sensors and radars. As said, this allows them to connect and interoperate with computers from other autonomous vehicles and/or roadside units. This implies that autonomous vehicles leave digital traces when they connect or interoperate. The data that comes from these digital traces can be used to develop new (to be determined) products or updates to enhance autonomous vehicles' <b style="color:#000;background:#66ffff">driving</b> ability or safety.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modularity">Modularity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Self-driving_car&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Modularity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Traditional vehicles and their accompanying technologies are manufactured as a product that will be complete, and unlike autonomous vehicles, they can only be improved if they are redesigned or reproduced. As said, autonomous vehicles are produced but due to their digital characteristics never finished. This is because autonomous vehicles are more modular since they are made up out of several modules which will be explained hereafter through a Layered Modular Architecture. The Layered Modular Architecture extends the architecture of purely physical vehicles by incorporating four loosely coupled layers of devices, networks, services and contents into Autonomous Vehicles. These loosely coupled layers can interact through certain standardized interfaces.
</p>
<ul><li>(1) The first layer of this architecture consists of the device layer. This layer consists of the following two parts: logical capability and physical machinery. The physical machinery refers to the actual vehicle itself (e.g. chassis and carrosserie). When it comes to digital technologies, the physical machinery is accompanied by a logical capability layer in the form of operating systems that helps to guide the vehicles itself and make it autonomous. The logical capability provides control over the vehicle and connects it with the other layers.;</li>
<li>(2) On top of the device layer comes the network layer. This layer also consists of two different parts: physical transport and logical transmission. The physical transport layer refers to the radars, sensors and cables of the autonomous vehicles which enable the transmission of digital information. Next to that, the network layer of autonomous vehicles also has a logical transmission which contains communication protocols and network standard to communicate the digital information with other networks and platforms or between layers. This increases the accessibility of the autonomous vehicles and enables the computational power of a network or platform.;</li>
<li>(3) The service layer contains the applications and their functionalities that serves the autonomous vehicle (and its owners) as they extract, create, store and consume content with regards to their own <b style="color:#000;background:#66ffff">driving</b> history, traffic congestion, roads or parking abilities for example.; and</li>
<li>(4) The final layer of the model is the contents layer. This layer contains the sounds, images and videos. The autonomous vehicles store, extract and use to act upon and improve their <b style="color:#000;background:#66ffff">driving</b> and understanding of the environment. The contents layer also provides metadata and directory information about the content's origin, ownership, copyright, encoding methods, content tags, geo-time stamps, and so on (Yoo et al., 2010).</li></ul>
<p>The consequence of layered modular architecture of autonomous vehicles (and other digital technologies) is that it enables the emergence and development of platforms and ecosystems around a product and/or certain modules of that product. Traditionally, automotive vehicles were developed, manufactured and maintained by traditional manufacturers. Nowadays app developers and content creators can help to develop more comprehensive product experience for the consumers which creates a platform around the product of autonomous vehicles.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Human_factor_challenges">Human factor challenges</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Self-driving_car&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Human factor challenges">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p><b style="color:#000;background:#ffff66">Self</b>-<b style="color:#000;background:#66ffff">driving</b> <b style="color:#000;background:#ffcc99">cars</b> are already exploring the difficulties of determining the intentions of pedestrians, bicyclists, and animals, and models of behavior must be programmed into <b style="color:#000;background:#66ffff">driving</b> algorithms.<sup id="cite_ref-:7_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:7-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> Human road users also have the challenge of determining the intentions of autonomous vehicles, where there is no driver with which to make eye contact or exchange hand signals. <a href="/wiki/Drive.ai" title="Drive.ai">Drive.ai</a> is testing a solution to this problem that involves LED signs mounted on the outside of the vehicle, announcing status such as "going now, don't cross" vs. "waiting for you to cross".<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Two human-factor challenges are important for safety. One is the handoff from automated <b style="color:#000;background:#66ffff">driving</b> to manual <b style="color:#000;background:#66ffff">driving</b>, which may become necessary due to unfavorable or unusual road conditions, or if the vehicle has limited capabilities. A sudden handoff could leave a human driver dangerously unprepared in the moment. In the long term, humans who have less practice at <b style="color:#000;background:#66ffff">driving</b> might have a lower skill level and thus be more dangerous in manual mode. The second challenge is known as <a href="/wiki/Risk_compensation" title="Risk compensation">risk compensation</a>: as a system is perceived to be safer, instead of benefiting entirely from all of the increased safety, people engage in riskier behavior and enjoy other benefits. Semi-automated <b style="color:#000;background:#ffcc99">cars</b> have been shown to suffer from this problem, for example with users of <a href="/wiki/Tesla_Autopilot" title="Tesla Autopilot">Tesla Autopilot</a> ignoring the road and using electronic devices or other activities against the advice of the company that the car is not capable of being completely autonomous. In the near future, pedestrians and bicyclists may travel in the street in a riskier fashion if they believe <b style="color:#000;background:#ffff66">self</b>-<b style="color:#000;background:#66ffff">driving</b> <b style="color:#000;background:#ffcc99">cars</b> are capable of avoiding them.
</p><p>In order for people to buy <b style="color:#000;background:#ffff66">self</b>-<b style="color:#000;background:#66ffff">driving</b> <b style="color:#000;background:#ffcc99">cars</b> and vote for the government to allow them on roads, the technology must be trusted as safe.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> <b style="color:#000;background:#ffff66">Self</b>-<b style="color:#000;background:#66ffff">driving</b> elevators were invented in 1900, but the high number of people refusing to use them slowed adoption for several decades until operator strikes increased demand and trust was built with advertising and features like the emergency stop button.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup>
<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Testing">Testing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Self-driving_car&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Testing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Waymo_self-driving_car_front_view.gk.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Waymo_self-driving_car_front_view.gk.jpg/220px-Waymo_self-driving_car_front_view.gk.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Waymo_self-driving_car_front_view.gk.jpg/330px-Waymo_self-driving_car_front_view.gk.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Waymo_self-driving_car_front_view.gk.jpg/440px-Waymo_self-driving_car_front_view.gk.jpg 2x" data-file-width="6000" data-file-height="4000" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Waymo_self-driving_car_front_view.gk.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A prototype of <a href="/wiki/Waymo" title="Waymo">Waymo</a>'s <b style="color:#000;background:#ffff66">self</b>-<b style="color:#000;background:#66ffff">driving</b> car, navigating public streets in <a href="/wiki/Mountain_View,_California" title="Mountain View, California">Mountain View, California</a> in 2017</div></div></div>
<p>The testing of vehicles with varying degrees of automation can be carried out either physically, in a closed environment<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> or, where permitted, on public roads (typically requiring a license or permit,<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> or adhering to a specific set of operating principles),<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> or in a virtual environment, i.e. using computer simulations.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup>
When driven on public roads, automated vehicles require a person to monitor their proper operation and "take over" when needed. For example, <a href="/wiki/New_York_(state)" title="New York (state)">New York state</a> has strict requirements for the test driver, such that the vehicle can be corrected at all times by a licensed operator; highlighted by Cardian Cube Company's application and discussions with New York State officials and the NYS DMV.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Apple_electric_car_project" title="Apple electric car project">Apple</a> is testing <b style="color:#000;background:#ffff66">self</b>-<b style="color:#000;background:#66ffff">driving</b> <b style="color:#000;background:#ffcc99">cars</b>, and has increased its fleet of test vehicles from three in April 2017, to 27 in January 2018, and 45 by March 2018.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Russian internet-company <a href="/wiki/Yandex" title="Yandex">Yandex</a> started to develop <a href="/wiki/Yandex_self-driving_car" title="Yandex self-driving car"><b style="color:#000;background:#ffff66">self</b>-<b style="color:#000;background:#66ffff">driving</b> <b style="color:#000;background:#ffcc99">cars</b></a> in early 2017. The first driverless prototype was launched in May 2017. In November 2017, Yandex released a video of its AV winter tests.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> The car drove successfully along snowy roads of Moscow. In June 2018, Yandex <b style="color:#000;background:#ffff66">self</b>-<b style="color:#000;background:#66ffff">driving</b> vehicle completed a 485-mile (780&#160;km) trip on a federal highway from Moscow to <a href="/wiki/Kazan" title="Kazan">Kazan</a> in autonomous mode.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> In August 2018, Yandex launched a Europe's first <a href="/wiki/Robo-Taxi" title="Robo-Taxi">robotaxi</a> service with no human driver behind the wheel in the Russian town of <a href="/wiki/Innopolis" title="Innopolis">Innopolis</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> At the beginning of 2020 it was reported that over 5,000 autonomous passenger rides were made in the city.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> At the end of 2018, Yandex obtained a license&#160;to operate autonomous vehicles on public roads in Nevada, USA. In 2019 and 2020, Yandex <b style="color:#000;background:#ffcc99">cars</b> carried out demo rides for <a href="/wiki/Consumer_Electronics_Show" title="Consumer Electronics Show">Consumer Electronic Show</a> visitors in Las Vegas. Yandex <b style="color:#000;background:#ffcc99">cars</b> were circulating the streets of the city without any human control.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> In 2019 Yandex started testing its <b style="color:#000;background:#ffff66">self</b>-<b style="color:#000;background:#66ffff">driving</b> <b style="color:#000;background:#ffcc99">cars</b> on the public roads of Israel.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> In October 2019, Yandex became one of the companies selected by <a href="/wiki/Michigan_Department_of_Transportation" title="Michigan Department of Transportation">Michigan Department of Transportation (MDOT)</a> to provide autonomous passenger rides to the visitors of Detroit Autoshow 2020.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> At the end of 2019, Yandex made an announcement its <b style="color:#000;background:#ffff66">self</b>-<b style="color:#000;background:#66ffff">driving</b> <b style="color:#000;background:#ffcc99">cars</b> passed 1 million miles in fully autonomous mode in Russia, Israel and USA.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> In February 2020, Yandex doubled its mileage with 2 million miles passed.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The progress of automated vehicles can be assessed by computing the average distance driven between "disengagements", when the automated system is switched off, typically by the intervention of a human driver. In 2017, Waymo reported 63 disengagements over 352,545&#160;mi (567,366&#160;km) of testing, an average distance of 5,596&#160;mi (9,006&#160;km) between disengagements, the highest among companies reporting such figures. Waymo also traveled a greater total distance than any of the other companies. Their 2017 rate of 0.18 disengagements per 1,000&#160;mi (1,600&#160;km) was an improvement over the 0.2 disengagements per 1,000&#160;mi (1,600&#160;km) in 2016, and 0.8 in 2015. In March 2017, Uber reported an average of just 0.67&#160;mi (1.08&#160;km) per disengagement. In the final three months of 2017, <a href="/wiki/Cruise_Automation" class="mw-redirect" title="Cruise Automation">Cruise</a> (now owned by <a href="/wiki/General_Motors" title="General Motors">GM</a>) averaged 5,224&#160;mi (8,407&#160;km) per disengagement over a total distance of 62,689&#160;mi (100,888&#160;km).<sup id="cite_ref-:6_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:6-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> In July 2018, the first electric driverless racing car, "Robocar", completed a 1.8-kilometer track, using its navigation system and artificial intelligence.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Fields_of_application">Fields of application</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Self-driving_car&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Fields of application">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Autonomous_trucks_and_vans">Autonomous trucks and vans</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Self-driving_car&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Autonomous trucks and vans">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Autonomous_truck" class="mw-redirect" title="Autonomous truck">Autonomous truck</a></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Online_food_ordering" title="Online food ordering">Online food ordering</a></div>
<p>Companies such as <a href="/wiki/Otto_(company)" title="Otto (company)">Otto</a> and <a href="/wiki/Starsky_Robotics" title="Starsky Robotics">Starsky Robotics</a> have focused on autonomous trucks. Automation of trucks is important, not only due to the improved safety aspects of these very heavy vehicles, but also due to the ability of fuel savings through <a href="/wiki/Platoon_(automobile)" title="Platoon (automobile)">platooning</a>.
</p><p>Autonomous vans are being used by <a href="/wiki/Online_grocer" title="Online grocer">online grocers</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Ocado" title="Ocado">Ocado</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Transport_systems">Transport systems</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Self-driving_car&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Transport systems">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In Europe, cities in Belgium, France, Italy and the UK are planning to operate transport systems for automated <b style="color:#000;background:#ffcc99">cars</b>,<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Detroit_News_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Detroit_News-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> and Germany, the Netherlands, and Spain have allowed public testing in traffic. In 2015, the UK launched public trials of the <a href="/wiki/LUTZ_Pathfinder" title="LUTZ Pathfinder">LUTZ Pathfinder</a> automated pod in <a href="/wiki/Milton_Keynes" title="Milton Keynes">Milton Keynes</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> Beginning in summer 2015, the French government allowed <a href="/wiki/PSA_Peugeot_Citro%C3%ABn" class="mw-redirect" title="PSA Peugeot Citroën">PSA Peugeot-Citroen</a> to make trials in real conditions in the Paris area. The experiments were planned to be extended to other cities such as Bordeaux and Strasbourg by 2016.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> The alliance between French companies <a href="/wiki/Thales_Group" title="Thales Group">THALES</a> and <a href="/wiki/Valeo" title="Valeo">Valeo</a> (provider of the first <b style="color:#000;background:#ffff66">self</b>-parking car system that equips Audi and Mercedes premi) is testing its own system.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> New Zealand is planning to use automated vehicles for public transport in Tauranga and Christchurch.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In China, <a href="/wiki/Baidu" title="Baidu">Baidu</a> and <a href="/wiki/King_Long" title="King Long">King Long</a> produce automated minibus, a vehicle with 14 seats, but without <b style="color:#000;background:#66ffff">driving</b> seat. With 100 vehicles produced, 2018 will be the first year with commercial automated service in China.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Impact">Impact</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Self-driving_car&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Impact">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>According to a 2020 study, <b style="color:#000;background:#ffff66">self</b>-<b style="color:#000;background:#66ffff">driving</b> <b style="color:#000;background:#ffcc99">cars</b> will increase productivity, and housing affordability, as well as reclaim land used for parking.<sup id="cite_ref-:9_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:9-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> However, <b style="color:#000;background:#ffff66">self</b>-<b style="color:#000;background:#66ffff">driving</b> <b style="color:#000;background:#ffcc99">cars</b> will cause greater energy use, traffic congestion and sprawl.<sup id="cite_ref-:9_110-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:9-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>According to a 2020 <i>Annual Review of Public Health</i> review of the literature, <b style="color:#000;background:#ffff66">self</b>-<b style="color:#000;background:#66ffff">driving</b> <b style="color:#000;background:#ffcc99">cars</b> "could increase some health risks (such as air pollution, noise, and sedentarism); however, if proper regulated, AVs will likely reduce morbidity and mortality from motor vehicle crashes and may help reshape cities to promote healthy urban environments."<sup id="cite_ref-Rojas_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rojas-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Public_health">Public health</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Self-driving_car&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Public health">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
