According to the law, the <a href="/wiki/Nevada_Department_of_Motor_Vehicles" title="Nevada Department of Motor Vehicles">Nevada Department of Motor Vehicles</a> is responsible for setting safety and performance standards and the agency is responsible for designating areas where automated <b style="color:#000;background:#ffcc99">cars</b> may be tested.<sup id="cite_ref-GCC0611_251-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GCC0611-251">&#91;251&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Forbes0611_252-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Forbes0611-252">&#91;252&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-253" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-253">&#91;253&#93;</a></sup> This legislation was supported by <a href="/wiki/Google" title="Google">Google</a> in an effort to legally conduct further testing of its <a href="/wiki/Google_driverless_car" class="mw-redirect" title="Google driverless car">Google driverless car</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-NYT0511_254-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYT0511-254">&#91;254&#93;</a></sup> The Nevada law defines an automated vehicle to be "a motor vehicle that uses <a href="/wiki/Artificial_intelligence" title="Artificial intelligence">artificial intelligence</a>, sensors and <a href="/wiki/Global_positioning_system" class="mw-redirect" title="Global positioning system">global positioning system</a> coordinates to drive itself without the active intervention of a human operator". The law also acknowledges that the operator will not need to pay attention while the car is operating itself. Google had further lobbied for an exemption from a ban on distracted <b style="color:#000;background:#66ffff">driving</b> to permit occupants to send <a href="/wiki/Text_message" class="mw-redirect" title="Text message">text messages</a> while sitting behind the wheel, but this did not become law.<sup id="cite_ref-NYT0511_254-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYT0511-254">&#91;254&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-255" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-255">&#91;255&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-256" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-256">&#91;256&#93;</a></sup> Furthermore, Nevada's regulations require a person behind the wheel and one in the passenger's seat during tests.<sup id="cite_ref-LVS_257-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LVS-257">&#91;257&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In April 2012, Florida became the second state to allow the testing of automated <b style="color:#000;background:#ffcc99">cars</b> on public roads, and California became the third when Governor <a href="/wiki/Jerry_Brown" title="Jerry Brown">Jerry Brown</a> signed the bill into law at Google Headquarters in <a href="/wiki/Mountain_View,_California" title="Mountain View, California">Mountain View</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-258" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-258">&#91;258&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-259" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-259">&#91;259&#93;</a></sup> In December 2013, Michigan became the fourth state to allow testing of driverless <b style="color:#000;background:#ffcc99">cars</b> on public roads.<sup id="cite_ref-260" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-260">&#91;260&#93;</a></sup> In July 2014, the city of <a href="/wiki/Coeur_d%27Alene,_Idaho" title="Coeur d&#39;Alene, Idaho">Coeur d'Alene, Idaho</a> adopted a robotics ordinance that includes provisions to allow for <b style="color:#000;background:#ffff66">self</b>-<b style="color:#000;background:#66ffff">driving</b> <b style="color:#000;background:#ffcc99">cars</b>.<sup id="cite_ref-261" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-261">&#91;261&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Jurvetson_Google_driverless_car_trimmed.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Jurvetson_Google_driverless_car_trimmed.jpg/220px-Jurvetson_Google_driverless_car_trimmed.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Jurvetson_Google_driverless_car_trimmed.jpg/330px-Jurvetson_Google_driverless_car_trimmed.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Jurvetson_Google_driverless_car_trimmed.jpg/440px-Jurvetson_Google_driverless_car_trimmed.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1800" data-file-height="1200" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Jurvetson_Google_driverless_car_trimmed.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Toyota_Prius" title="Toyota Prius">Toyota Prius</a> modified by Google to operate as a driverless car</div></div></div>
<p>On 19 February 2016, <a href="/wiki/California_Assembly" class="mw-redirect" title="California Assembly">California Assembly</a> Bill 2866 was introduced in California that would allow automated vehicles to operate on public roads, including those without a driver, steering wheel, accelerator pedal, or brake pedal. The bill states that the <a href="/wiki/California_Department_of_Motor_Vehicles" title="California Department of Motor Vehicles">California Department of Motor Vehicles</a> would need to comply with these regulations by 1 July 2018 for these rules to take effect. As of November&#160;2016<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Self-driving_car&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>, this bill has yet to pass the house of origin.<sup id="cite_ref-262" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-262">&#91;262&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In September 2016, the US Department of Transportation released its Federal Automated Vehicles Policy, and California published discussions on the subject in October 2016.<sup id="cite_ref-263" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-263">&#91;263&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-264" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-264">&#91;264&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In December 2016, the California Department of Motor Vehicles ordered <a href="/wiki/Uber" title="Uber">Uber</a> to remove its <b style="color:#000;background:#ffff66">self</b>-<b style="color:#000;background:#66ffff">driving</b> vehicles from the road in response to two red-light violations. Uber immediately blamed the violations on human-error, and has suspended the drivers.<sup id="cite_ref-265" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-265">&#91;265&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Legislation_in_Europe">Legislation in Europe</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Self-driving_car&amp;action=edit&amp;section=50" title="Edit section: Legislation in Europe">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In 2013, the <a href="/wiki/Government_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Government of the United Kingdom">government of the United Kingdom</a> permitted the testing of automated <b style="color:#000;background:#ffcc99">cars</b> on public roads.<sup id="cite_ref-UKTEST2013_266-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UKTEST2013-266">&#91;266&#93;</a></sup> Before this, all testing of robotic vehicles in the UK had been conducted on private property.<sup id="cite_ref-UKTEST2013_266-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UKTEST2013-266">&#91;266&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2014, the <a href="/wiki/Government_of_France" title="Government of France">Government of France</a> announced that testing of automated <b style="color:#000;background:#ffcc99">cars</b> on public roads would be allowed in 2015. 2000&#160;km of road would be opened through the national territory, especially in Bordeaux, in Isère, Île-de-France and Strasbourg. At the 2015 ITS World Congress, a conference dedicated to intelligent transport systems, the very first demonstration of automated vehicles on open road in France was carried out in Bordeaux in early October 2015.<sup id="cite_ref-267" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-267">&#91;267&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2015, a preemptive lawsuit against various automobile companies such as GM, Ford, and Toyota accused them of "Hawking vehicles that are vulnerable to hackers who could hypothetically wrest control of essential functions such as brakes and steering."<sup id="cite_ref-268" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-268">&#91;268&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In spring of 2015, the Federal Department of Environment, Transport, Energy and Communications in Switzerland (UVEK) allowed <a href="/wiki/Swisscom" title="Swisscom">Swisscom</a> to test a driverless <a href="/wiki/Volkswagen_Passat" title="Volkswagen Passat">Volkswagen Passat</a> on the streets of <a href="/wiki/Zurich" class="mw-redirect" title="Zurich">Zurich</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Swisstest2015_269-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Swisstest2015-269">&#91;269&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>As of April 2017, it is possible to conduct public road tests for development vehicles in <a href="/wiki/Hungary" title="Hungary">Hungary</a>, furthermore the construction of a closed test track, the ZalaZone test track,<sup id="cite_ref-270" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-270">&#91;270&#93;</a></sup> suitable for testing highly automated functions is also under way near the city of <a href="/wiki/Zalaegerszeg" title="Zalaegerszeg">Zalaegerszeg</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-271" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-271">&#91;271&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Legislation_in_Asia">Legislation in Asia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Self-driving_car&amp;action=edit&amp;section=51" title="Edit section: Legislation in Asia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In 2016, the Singapore Land Transit Authority in partnership with UK automotive supplier Delphi Automotive, began launch preparations for a test run of a fleet of <a href="/wiki/Automated_taxi" class="mw-redirect" title="Automated taxi">automated taxis</a> for an on-demand automated cab service to take effect in 2017.<sup id="cite_ref-272" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-272">&#91;272&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2017, the South Korean government stated that the lack of universal standards is preventing its own legislation from pushing new domestic rules. However, once the international standards are settled, South Korea's legislation will resemble the international standards.<sup id="cite_ref-273" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-273">&#91;273&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Liability">Liability</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Self-driving_car&amp;action=edit&amp;section=52" title="Edit section: Liability">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Self-driving_car_liability" title="Self-driving car liability"><b style="color:#000;background:#ffff66">Self</b>-<b style="color:#000;background:#66ffff">driving</b> car liability</a></div>
<p><b style="color:#000;background:#ffff66">Self</b>-<b style="color:#000;background:#66ffff">driving</b> car liability is a developing area of law and policy that will determine who is liable when an automated car causes physical damage to persons, or breaks road rules.<sup id="cite_ref-:5_1-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:5-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-274" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-274">&#91;274&#93;</a></sup> When automated <b style="color:#000;background:#ffcc99">cars</b> shift the control of <b style="color:#000;background:#66ffff">driving</b> from humans to automated car technology, there may be a need for existing liability laws to evolve in order to fairly identify the parties responsible for damage and injury, and to address the potential for conflicts of interest between human occupants, system operator, insurers, and the public purse.<sup id="cite_ref-RAND_130-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RAND-130">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup> Increases in the use of automated car technologies (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Advanced_driver-assistance_systems" title="Advanced driver-assistance systems">advanced driver-assistance systems</a>) may prompt incremental shifts in this responsibility for <b style="color:#000;background:#66ffff">driving</b>. It is claimed by proponents to have potential to affect the frequency of road accidents, although it is difficult to assess this claim in the absence of data from substantial actual use.<sup id="cite_ref-275" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-275">&#91;275&#93;</a></sup> If there was a dramatic improvement in safety, the operators may seek to project their liability for the remaining accidents onto others as part of their reward for the improvement.  However, there is no obvious reason why they should escape liability if any such effects were found to be modest or nonexistent, since part of the purpose of such liability is to give an incentive to the party controlling something to do whatever is necessary to avoid it causing harm. Potential users may be reluctant to trust an operator if it seeks to pass its normal liability on to others.
</p><p>In any case, a well-advised person who is not controlling a car at all (Level 5) would be understandably reluctant to accept liability for something out of their control. And when there is some degree of sharing control possible (Level 3 or 4), a well-advised person would be concerned that the vehicle might try to pass back control at the last seconds before an accident, to pass responsibility and liability back too, but in circumstances where the potential driver has no better prospects of avoiding the crash than the vehicle, since they have not necessarily been paying close attention, and if it is too hard for the very smart car it might be too hard for a human. Since operators, especially those familiar with trying to ignore existing legal obligations (under a motto like 'seek forgiveness, not permission'), such as Waymo or Uber, could be normally expected to try to avoid responsibility to the maximum degree possible, there is potential for attempt to let the operators evade being held liable for accidents while they are in control.
</p><p>As higher levels of automation are commercially introduced (Level 3 and 4), the insurance industry may see a greater proportion of commercial and product liability lines while personal automobile insurance shrinks.<sup id="cite_ref-KPMG_276-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-KPMG-276">&#91;276&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>When it comes to the direction of fully autonomous car liability, torts cannot be ignored. In any car accident the issue of negligence usually arises. In the situation of autonomous <b style="color:#000;background:#ffcc99">cars</b>, negligence would most likely fall on the manufacturer because it would be hard to pin a breach of duty of care on the user who isn't in control of the vehicle. The only time negligence was brought up in an autonomous car lawsuit, there was a settlement between the person struck by the autonomous vehicle and the manufacturer (General Motors).<sup id="cite_ref-277" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-277">&#91;277&#93;</a></sup> Next, product liability would most likely cause liability to fall on the manufacturer. For an accident to fall under product liability, there needs to be either a defect, failure to provide adequate warnings, or foreseeability by the manufacturer.<sup id="cite_ref-278" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-278">&#91;278&#93;</a></sup> Third, is strict liability which in this case is similar to product liability based on the design defect. Based on a Nevada Supreme Court ruling (Ford vs. Trejo) the plaintiff needs to prove failure of the manufacturer to pass the consumer expectation test.<sup id="cite_ref-279" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-279">&#91;279&#93;</a></sup> That is potentially how the three major torts could function when it comes to autonomous car liability.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Vehicle_communication_systems">Vehicle communication systems</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Self-driving_car&amp;action=edit&amp;section=53" title="Edit section: Vehicle communication systems">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Vehicular_communication_systems" title="Vehicular communication systems">Vehicular communication systems</a></div><p>Vehicle networking may be desirable due to difficulty with computer vision being able to recognize brake lights, turn signals, buses, and similar things. However, the usefulness of such systems would be diminished by the fact current <b style="color:#000;background:#ffcc99">cars</b> are equipped with them; they may also pose privacy concerns.<sup id="cite_ref-:8_236-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:8-236">&#91;236&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Individual vehicles may benefit from information obtained from other vehicles in the vicinity, especially information relating to traffic congestion and safety hazards. Vehicular communication systems use vehicles and roadside units as the communicating <a href="/wiki/Node_(networking)" title="Node (networking)">nodes</a> in a peer-to-peer network, providing each other with information. As a cooperative approach, vehicular communication systems can allow all cooperating vehicles to be more effective. According to a 2010 study by the <a href="/wiki/US_National_Highway_Traffic_Safety_Administration" class="mw-redirect" title="US National Highway Traffic Safety Administration">US National Highway Traffic Safety Administration</a>, vehicular communication systems could help avoid up to 79% of all traffic accidents.<sup id="cite_ref-280" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-280">&#91;280&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>There have so far been no complete implementation of peer-to-peer networking on the scale required for traffic: each individual vehicle would have to connect with potentially hundreds of different vehicles that could be going in and out of range.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>In 2012, computer scientists at the University of Texas in Austin began developing smart intersections designed for automated <b style="color:#000;background:#ffcc99">cars</b>. The intersections will have no traffic lights and no stop signs, instead using computer programs that will communicate directly with each car on the road.<sup id="cite_ref-281" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-281">&#91;281&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2017, Researchers from Arizona State University developed a 1/10 scale intersection and proposed an intersection management technique called Crossroads. It was shown that Crossroads is very resilient to network delay of both V2I communication and <a href="/wiki/Worst-case_execution_time" title="Worst-case execution time">Worst-case Execution time</a> of the intersection manager.<sup id="cite_ref-282" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-282">&#91;282&#93;</a></sup> In 2018, a robust approach was introduced which is resilient to both model mismatch and external disturbances such as wind and bumps.<sup id="cite_ref-283" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-283">&#91;283&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Among connected <b style="color:#000;background:#ffcc99">cars</b>, an unconnected one is the weakest link and will be increasingly banned from busy high-speed roads, as predicted by the <a href="/wiki/Helsinki" title="Helsinki">Helsinki</a> think tank, Nordic Communications Corporation, in January 2016.<sup id="cite_ref-284" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-284">&#91;284&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Public_opinion_surveys">Public opinion surveys</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Self-driving_car&amp;action=edit&amp;section=54" title="Edit section: Public opinion surveys">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<table class="box-Overly_detailed plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-style ambox-overly_detailed" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f2/Edit-clear.svg/40px-Edit-clear.svg.png" decoding="async" width="40" height="40" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f2/Edit-clear.svg/60px-Edit-clear.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f2/Edit-clear.svg/80px-Edit-clear.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="48" data-file-height="48" /></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This article <b>may contain an excessive amount of intricate detail that may interest only a particular audience</b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help by <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Content_forking#Article_spinoffs:_.22Summary_style.22_meta-articles_and_summary_sections" title="Wikipedia:Content forking">spinning off</a> or <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Handling_trivia#Recommendations_for_handling_trivia" title="Wikipedia:Handling trivia">relocating</a> any relevant information, and removing excessive detail that may be against <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:What_Wikipedia_is_not" title="Wikipedia:What Wikipedia is not">Wikipedia's inclusion policy</a>.</span>  <small class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">August 2016</span>)</i></small><small class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table><p>In a 2011 online survey of 2,006 US and UK consumers by <a href="/wiki/Accenture" title="Accenture">Accenture</a>, 49% said they would be comfortable using a "driverless car".<sup id="cite_ref-285" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-285">&#91;285&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A 2012 survey of 17,400 vehicle owners by <a href="/wiki/J.D._Power_and_Associates" class="mw-redirect" title="J.D. Power and Associates">J.D. Power and Associates</a> found 37% initially said they would be interested in purchasing a "fully autonomous car". However, that figure dropped to 20% if told the technology would cost US$3,000 more.<sup id="cite_ref-286" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-286">&#91;286&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In a 2012 survey of about 1,000 German drivers by automotive researcher Puls, 22% of the respondents had a positive attitude towards these <b style="color:#000;background:#ffcc99">cars</b>, 10% were undecided, 44% were skeptical and 24% were hostile.<sup id="cite_ref-287" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-287">&#91;287&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A 2013 survey of 1,500 consumers across 10 countries by <a href="/wiki/Cisco_Systems" title="Cisco Systems">Cisco Systems</a> found 57% "stated they would be likely to ride in a car controlled entirely by technology that does not require a human driver", with Brazil, India and China the most willing to trust automated technology.<sup id="cite_ref-288" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-288">&#91;288&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In a 2014 US telephone survey by <a href="/w/index.php?title=Insurance.com&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Insurance.com (page does not exist)">Insurance.com</a>, over three-quarters of licensed drivers said they would at least consider buying a <b style="color:#000;background:#ffff66">self</b>-<b style="color:#000;background:#66ffff">driving</b> car, rising to 86% if car insurance were cheaper. 31.7% said they would not continue to drive once an automated car was available instead.<sup id="cite_ref-289" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-289">&#91;289&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In a February 2015 survey of top auto journalists, 46% predict that either Tesla or Daimler will be the first to the market with a fully autonomous vehicle, while (at 38%) Daimler is predicted to be the most functional, safe, and in-demand autonomous vehicle.<sup id="cite_ref-290" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-290">&#91;290&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2015 a questionnaire survey by Delft University of Technology explored the opinion of 5,000 people from 109 countries on automated <b style="color:#000;background:#66ffff">driving</b>. Results showed that respondents, on average, found manual <b style="color:#000;background:#66ffff">driving</b> the most enjoyable mode of <b style="color:#000;background:#66ffff">driving</b>. 22% of the respondents did not want to spend any money for a fully automated <b style="color:#000;background:#66ffff">driving</b> system. Respondents were found to be most concerned about software hacking/misuse, and were also concerned about legal issues and safety. Finally, respondents from more developed countries (in terms of lower accident statistics, higher education, and higher income) were less comfortable with their vehicle transmitting data.<sup id="cite_ref-:4_291-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:4-291">&#91;291&#93;</a></sup> The survey also gave results on potential consumer opinion on interest of purchasing an automated car, stating that 37% of surveyed current owners were either "definitely" or "probably" interested in purchasing an automated car.<sup id="cite_ref-:4_291-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:4-291">&#91;291&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2016, a survey in Germany examined the opinion of 1,603 people, who were representative in terms of age, gender, and education for the German population, towards partially, highly, and fully automated <b style="color:#000;background:#ffcc99">cars</b>. Results showed that men and women differ in their willingness to use them. Men felt less anxiety and more joy towards automated <b style="color:#000;background:#ffcc99">cars</b>, whereas women showed the exact opposite. The gender difference towards anxiety was especially pronounced between young men and women but decreased with participants' age.<sup id="cite_ref-292" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-292">&#91;292&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2016, a <a href="/wiki/PwC" class="mw-redirect" title="PwC">PwC</a> survey, in the United States, showing the opinion of 1,584 people, highlights that "66 percent of respondents said they think autonomous <b style="color:#000;background:#ffcc99">cars</b> are probably smarter than the average human driver". People are still worried about safety and mostly the fact of having the car hacked. Nevertheless, only 13% of the interviewees see no advantages in this new kind of <b style="color:#000;background:#ffcc99">cars</b>.<sup id="cite_ref-293" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-293">&#91;293&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A Pew Research Center survey of 4,135 US adults conducted 1–15 May 2017 finds that many Americans anticipate significant impacts from various automation technologies in the course of their lifetimes—from the widespread adoption of automated vehicles to the replacement of entire job categories with robot workers.<sup id="cite_ref-294" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-294">&#91;294&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Results from two opinion surveys of 54 and 187 US adults respectively were published in 2019. A new standardised questionnaire, the autonomous vehicle acceptance model (AVAM) was developed, including additional description to help respondents better understand the implications of different automation levels. Results showed that users were less accepting of high autonomy levels and displayed significantly lower intention to use highly autonomous vehicles. Additionally, partial autonomy (regardless of level) was perceived as requiring uniformly higher driver engagement (usage of hands, feet and eyes) than full autonomy.<sup id="cite_ref-295" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-295">&#91;295&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Moral_issues">Moral issues</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Self-driving_car&amp;action=edit&amp;section=55" title="Edit section: Moral issues">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Machine_ethics" title="Machine ethics">Machine ethics</a></div>
<table class="box-Tone plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-style ambox-Tone" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f2/Edit-clear.svg/40px-Edit-clear.svg.png" decoding="async" width="40" height="40" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f2/Edit-clear.svg/60px-Edit-clear.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f2/Edit-clear.svg/80px-Edit-clear.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="48" data-file-height="48" /></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section's <b>tone or style may not reflect the <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Writing_better_articles#Tone" title="Wikipedia:Writing better articles">encyclopedic tone</a> used on Wikipedia</b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> See Wikipedia's <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Writing_better_articles#Tone" title="Wikipedia:Writing better articles">guide to writing better articles</a> for suggestions.</span>  <small class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">February 2019</span>)</i></small><small class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>With the emergence of automated automobiles, various ethical issues arise. While the introduction of automated vehicles to the mass market is said to be inevitable due to a presumed but untestable potential for reduction of crashes by "up to" 90%<sup id="cite_ref-296" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-296">&#91;296&#93;</a></sup> and their potential greater accessibility to disabled, elderly, and young passengers, a range of ethical issues have not been fully addressed. Those include, but are not limited to: the moral, financial, and criminal responsibility for crashes and breaches of law; the decisions a car is to make right before a (fatal) crash; privacy issues including potential for mass surveillance; potential for massive job losses and unemployment among drivers; de-skilling and loss of independence by vehicle users; exposure to hacking and malware; and the further concentration of market and data power in the hands of a few global conglomerates capable of consolidating AI capacity, and of lobbying governments to facilitate the shift of liability onto others and their potential destruction of existing occupations and industries.
</p><p>There are different opinions on who should be held liable in case of a crash, especially with people being hurt. Many experts see the car manufacturers themselves responsible for those crashes that occur due to a technical malfunction or misconstruction.<sup id="cite_ref-:2_297-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-297">&#91;297&#93;</a></sup> Besides the fact that the car manufacturer would be the source of the problem in a situation where a car crashes due to a technical issue, there is another important reason why car manufacturers could be held responsible: it would encourage them to innovate and heavily invest into fixing those issues, not only due to protection of the brand image, but also due to financial and criminal consequences. However, there are also voices<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too-vague attribution or weasel words. (February 2019)">who?</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> that argue those using or owning the vehicle should be held responsible since they know the risks involved in using such a vehicle. Experts<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too-vague attribution or weasel words. (February 2019)">who?</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> suggest introducing a tax or insurances that would protect owners and users of automated vehicles of claims made by victims of an accident.<sup id="cite_ref-:2_297-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-297">&#91;297&#93;</a></sup> Other possible parties that can be held responsible in case of a technical failure include <a href="/wiki/Software_engineer" title="Software engineer">software engineers</a> that programmed the code for the automated operation of the vehicles, and suppliers of components of the AV.<sup id="cite_ref-298" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-298">&#91;298&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Taking aside the question of legal liability and moral responsibility, the question arises how automated vehicles should be programmed to behave in an emergency situation where either passengers or other traffic participants like: pedestrians, bicyclists and other drivers are endangered. A moral dilemma that a software engineer or car manufacturer might face in programming the operating software is described in an ethical thought experiment, the trolley problem: a conductor of a trolley has the choice of staying on the planned track and running over five people, or turn the trolley onto a track where it would kill only one person, assuming there is no traffic on it.<sup id="cite_ref-299" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-299">&#91;299&#93;</a></sup> When a <b style="color:#000;background:#ffff66">self</b>-<b style="color:#000;background:#66ffff">driving</b> car is in following scenario: it's <b style="color:#000;background:#66ffff">driving</b> with passengers and suddenly a person appears in its way. The car has to decide between the two options, either to run the person over or to avoid hitting the person by swerving into a wall, killing the passengers.<sup id="cite_ref-300" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-300">&#91;300&#93;</a></sup> There are two main considerations that need to be addressed. First, what moral basis would be used by an automated vehicle to make decisions? Second, how could those be translated into software code? Researchers have suggested, in particular, two ethical theories to be applicable to the behavior of automated vehicles in cases of emergency: <a href="/wiki/Deontological_ethics" title="Deontological ethics">deontology</a> and <a href="/wiki/Utilitarianism" title="Utilitarianism">utilitarianism</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-:7_7-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:7-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:3_301-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3-301">&#91;301&#93;</a></sup> Asimov's <a href="/wiki/Three_Laws_of_Robotics" title="Three Laws of Robotics">Three Laws of Robotics</a> are a typical example of deontological ethics. The theory suggests that an automated car needs to follow strict written-out rules that it needs to follow in any situation. Utilitarianism suggests the idea that any decision must be made based on the goal to maximize utility. This needs a definition of utility which could be maximizing the number of people surviving in a crash. Critics suggest that automated vehicles should adapt a mix of multiple theories to be able to respond morally right in the instance of a crash.<sup id="cite_ref-:7_7-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:7-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:3_301-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3-301">&#91;301&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Many 'trolley' discussions skip over the practical problems of how a probabilistic machine learning vehicle AI could be sophisticated enough to understand that a deep problem of moral philosophy is presenting itself from instant to instant while using a dynamic projection into the near future, what sort of moral problem it actually would be if any, what the relevant weightings in human value terms should be given to all the other humans involved who will be probably unreliably identified, and how reliably it can assess the probable outcomes. These practical difficulties, and those around testing and assessment of solutions to them, may present as much of a challenge as the theoretical abstractions.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>While most trolley conundrums involve hyperbolic and unlikely fact patterns, it is inevitable mundane ethical decisions and risk calculations such as the precise millisecond a car should yield to a yellow light or how closely to drive to a bike lane will need to be programmed into the software of autonomous vehicles.<sup id="cite_ref-:7_7-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:7-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_302-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-302">&#91;302&#93;</a></sup> Algorithms dictate, for example, how closely to drive to a bike lane or the precise moment an autonomous car should yield to a yellow light.<sup id="cite_ref-:7_7-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:7-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_302-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-302">&#91;302&#93;</a></sup> Mundane ethical situations may even be more relevant than rare fatal circumstances because of the specificity implicated and their large scope.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_302-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-302">&#91;302&#93;</a></sup> Mundane situations involving drivers and pedestrians are so prevalent that, in the aggregate, produce large amounts of injuries and deaths.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_302-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-302">&#91;302&#93;</a></sup> Hence, even incremental permutations of moral algorithms can have a notable effect when considered in their entirety.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_302-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-302">&#91;302&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Privacy-related issues arise mainly from the interconnectivity of automated <b style="color:#000;background:#ffcc99">cars</b>, making it just another mobile device that can gather any information about an individual. This information gathering ranges from tracking of the routes taken, voice recording, video recording, preferences in media that is consumed in the car, behavioral patterns, to many more streams of information.<sup id="cite_ref-:8_236-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:8-236">&#91;236&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-303" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-303">&#91;303&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-304" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-304">&#91;304&#93;</a></sup> The data and communications infrastructure needed to support these vehicles may also be capable of surveillance, especially if coupled to other data sets and advanced analytics.<sup id="cite_ref-:8_236-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:8-236">&#91;236&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The implementation of automated vehicles to the mass market might cost up to 5 million jobs in the US alone, making up almost 3% of the workforce.<sup id="cite_ref-305" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-305">&#91;305&#93;</a></sup> Those jobs include drivers of taxis, buses, vans, trucks, and e-hailing vehicles. Many industries, such as the auto insurance industry are indirectly affected. This industry alone generates an annual revenue of about US$220 billion, supporting 277,000 jobs.<sup id="cite_ref-306" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-306">&#91;306&#93;</a></sup> To put this into perspective–this is about the number of mechanical engineering jobs.<sup id="cite_ref-307" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-307">&#91;307&#93;</a></sup> The potential loss of a majority of those jobs will have a tremendous impact on those individuals involved.<sup id="cite_ref-308" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-308">&#91;308&#93;</a></sup> Both India and China have placed bans on automated <b style="color:#000;background:#ffcc99">cars</b> with the former citing protection of jobs.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Massachusetts_Institute_of_Technology" title="Massachusetts Institute of Technology">Massachusetts Institute of Technology</a> (MIT) has animated the trolley problem in the context of autonomous <b style="color:#000;background:#ffcc99">cars</b> in a website called The Moral Machine.<sup id="cite_ref-The_Moral_Machine_Experiment_309-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_Moral_Machine_Experiment-309">&#91;309&#93;</a></sup> The Moral Machine generates random scenarios in which autonomous <b style="color:#000;background:#ffcc99">cars</b> malfunction and forces the user to choose between two harmful courses of action.<sup id="cite_ref-The_Moral_Machine_Experiment_309-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_Moral_Machine_Experiment-309">&#91;309&#93;</a></sup> MIT's Moral Machine experiment has collected data involving over 40 million decisions from people in 233 countries to ascertain peoples' moral preferences.  The MIT study illuminates that ethical preferences vary among cultures and demographics and likely correlate with modern institutions and geographic traits.<sup id="cite_ref-The_Moral_Machine_Experiment_309-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_Moral_Machine_Experiment-309">&#91;309&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Global trends of the MIT study highlight that, overall, people prefer to save the lives of humans over other animals, prioritize the lives of many rather than few, and spare the lives of young rather than old.<sup id="cite_ref-The_Moral_Machine_Experiment_309-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_Moral_Machine_Experiment-309">&#91;309&#93;</a></sup> Men are slightly more likely to spare the lives of women, and religious affiliates are slightly more likely to prioritize human life.  The lives of criminals were prioritized more than cats, but the lives of dogs were prioritized more than the lives of criminals.<sup id="cite_ref-9_May_2019_310-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9_May_2019-310">&#91;310&#93;</a></sup> The lives of homeless were spared more than the elderly, but the lives of homeless were spared less often than the obese.<sup id="cite_ref-9_May_2019_310-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9_May_2019-310">&#91;310&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>People overwhelmingly express a preference for autonomous vehicles to be programmed with utilitarian ideas, that is, in a manner that generates the least harm and minimizes <b style="color:#000;background:#66ffff">driving</b> casualties.<sup id="cite_ref-Jean-François_Bonnefon_1510_311-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jean-François_Bonnefon_1510-311">&#91;311&#93;</a></sup> While people want others to purchase utilitarian promoting vehicles, they themselves prefer to ride in vehicles that prioritize the lives of people inside the vehicle at all costs.<sup id="cite_ref-Jean-François_Bonnefon_1510_311-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jean-François_Bonnefon_1510-311">&#91;311&#93;</a></sup> This presents a paradox in which people prefer that others drive utilitarian vehicles designed to maximize the lives preserved in a fatal situation but want to ride in <b style="color:#000;background:#ffcc99">cars</b> that prioritize the safety of passengers at all costs.<sup id="cite_ref-Jean-François_Bonnefon_1510_311-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jean-François_Bonnefon_1510-311">&#91;311&#93;</a></sup> People disapprove of regulations that promote utilitarian views and would be less willing to purchase a <b style="color:#000;background:#ffff66">self</b>-<b style="color:#000;background:#66ffff">driving</b> car that may opt to promote the greatest good at the expense of its passengers.<sup id="cite_ref-Jean-François_Bonnefon_1510_311-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jean-François_Bonnefon_1510-311">&#91;311&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Bonnefon et al. conclude that the regulation of autonomous vehicle ethical prescriptions may be counterproductive to societal safety.<sup id="cite_ref-Jean-François_Bonnefon_1510_311-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jean-François_Bonnefon_1510-311">&#91;311&#93;</a></sup> This is because, if the government mandates utilitarian ethics and people prefer to ride in <b style="color:#000;background:#ffff66">self</b>-protective <b style="color:#000;background:#ffcc99">cars</b>, it could prevent the large scale implementation of <b style="color:#000;background:#ffff66">self</b>-<b style="color:#000;background:#66ffff">driving</b> <b style="color:#000;background:#ffcc99">cars</b>.<sup id="cite_ref-Jean-François_Bonnefon_1510_311-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jean-François_Bonnefon_1510-311">&#91;311&#93;</a></sup> Delaying the adoption of autonomous <b style="color:#000;background:#ffcc99">cars</b> vitiates the safety of society as a whole because this technology is projected to save so many lives.<sup id="cite_ref-Jean-François_Bonnefon_1510_311-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jean-François_Bonnefon_1510-311">&#91;311&#93;</a></sup> This is a paradigmatic example of the tragedy of the commons in which rational actors cater to their <b style="color:#000;background:#ffff66">self</b>-interested preferences at the expense of societal utility.<sup id="cite_ref-312" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-312">&#91;312&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Anticipated_launch_of_cars">Anticipated launch of <b style="color:#000;background:#ffcc99">cars</b></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Self-driving_car&amp;action=edit&amp;section=56" title="Edit section: Anticipated launch of cars">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Lane_centering#Sample_of_level_2_automated_cars" title="Lane centering">Lane centering §&#160;Sample of level 2 automated <b style="color:#000;background:#ffcc99">cars</b></a></div>
<p>In December 2015, <a href="/wiki/Tesla,_Inc." title="Tesla, Inc.">Tesla</a> CEO <a href="/wiki/Elon_Musk" title="Elon Musk">Elon Musk</a> predicted that a completely automated car would be introduced by the end of 2018;<sup id="cite_ref-313" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-313">&#91;313&#93;</a></sup> in December 2017, he announced that it would take another two years to launch a fully <b style="color:#000;background:#ffff66">self</b>-<b style="color:#000;background:#66ffff">driving</b> Tesla onto the market.<sup id="cite_ref-314" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-314">&#91;314&#93;</a></sup>
<a href="/wiki/Waymo" title="Waymo">Waymo</a> launched a ride hailing service in Phoenix in December, 2018. <a href="/wiki/Drive.ai" title="Drive.ai">Drive.ai</a> is doing a trial run in Frisco, Texas and Arlington Texas.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>In March 2019, ahead of the autonomous racing series <a href="/wiki/Roborace" title="Roborace">Roborace</a>, Robocar set the <a href="/wiki/Guinness_World_Records" title="Guinness World Records">Guinness World Record</a> for being the fastest autonomous car in the world. In pushing the limits of <b style="color:#000;background:#ffff66">self</b>-<b style="color:#000;background:#66ffff">driving</b> vehicles, Robocar reached 282.42 kph (175.49 mph) – an average confirmed by the UK Timing Association at Elvington in Yorkshire, UK. <sup id="cite_ref-315" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-315">&#91;315&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="In_fiction">In fiction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Self-driving_car&amp;action=edit&amp;section=57" title="Edit section: In fiction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<table class="box-Refexample plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><a href="/wiki/File:Ambox_important.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/b4/Ambox_important.svg/40px-Ambox_important.svg.png" decoding="async" width="40" height="40" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/b4/Ambox_important.svg/60px-Ambox_important.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/b4/Ambox_important.svg/80px-Ambox_important.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="40" data-file-height="40" /></a></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>gives <a href="/wiki/Template:Refexample#Self-sourcing_examples" title="Template:Refexample"><b style="color:#000;background:#ffff66">self</b>-sourcing popular culture examples</a> without describing their significance in the context of the article.</b><span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Self-driving_car&amp;action=edit">improve this section</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing_with_Wiki_Markup/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing with Wiki Markup/1">adding citations to reliable sources</a> that describe the examples' significance, and by removing less pertinent examples. <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">Unsourced</a> or poorly sourced material may be challenged or removed.</span>  <small class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">February 2018</span>)</i></small><small class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Lexus_2054_Minority_Report_concept.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Lexus_2054_Minority_Report_concept.jpg/220px-Lexus_2054_Minority_Report_concept.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Lexus_2054_Minority_Report_concept.jpg/330px-Lexus_2054_Minority_Report_concept.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Lexus_2054_Minority_Report_concept.jpg/440px-Lexus_2054_Minority_Report_concept.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1152" data-file-height="864" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Lexus_2054_Minority_Report_concept.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/Minority_Report_(film)" title="Minority Report (film)">Minority Report's</a></i> <a href="/wiki/Lexus_2054" title="Lexus 2054">Lexus 2054</a> on display in <a href="/wiki/Paris" title="Paris">Paris</a> in October 2002</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_film">In film</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Self-driving_car&amp;action=edit&amp;section=58" title="Edit section: In film">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The automated and occasionally sentient <b style="color:#000;background:#ffff66">self</b>-<b style="color:#000;background:#66ffff">driving</b> car story has earned its place in both literary science fiction and pop sci-fi.<sup id="cite_ref-316" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-316">&#91;316&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>A <a href="/wiki/VW_Beetle" class="mw-redirect" title="VW Beetle">VW Beetle</a> named <a href="/w/index.php?title=Dudu_(car)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Dudu (car) (page does not exist)"><i>Dudu</i></a><sup class="noprint" style="font-style: normal;">&#160;&#91;<a href="https://de.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dudu" class="extiw" title="de:Dudu">de</a>&#93;</sup> features in the 1971 to 1978 German <a href="/wiki/Superbug_(film_series)" title="Superbug (film series)">Superbug film series</a>, similar to <a href="/wiki/Disney" class="mw-redirect" title="Disney">Disney</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Herbie" title="Herbie">Herbie</a>, but with an electronic brain. (Herbie, also a Beetle, was instead depicted as an <a href="/wiki/Anthropomorphic" class="mw-redirect" title="Anthropomorphic">anthropomorphic</a> car with its own spirit.)</li>
<li>In the film <i><a href="/wiki/Batman_(1989_film)" title="Batman (1989 film)">Batman</a></i> (1989), starring <a href="/wiki/Michael_Keaton" title="Michael Keaton">Michael Keaton</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Batmobile" title="Batmobile">Batmobile</a> is shown to be able to drive to <a href="/wiki/Batman" title="Batman">Batman</a>'s current location with some navigation commands from Batman and possibly some automation. In the 1992 sequel <i><a href="/wiki/Batman_Returns" title="Batman Returns">Batman Returns</a></i> the Batmobile's <b style="color:#000;background:#ffff66">self</b>-<b style="color:#000;background:#66ffff">driving</b> system is hijacked by <a href="/wiki/Penguin_(character)" title="Penguin (character)">The Penguin</a>, who wreaks havoc through the city to frame Batman until Bruce undoes the sabotage.</li>
<li>The film <i><a href="/wiki/Total_Recall_(1990_film)" title="Total Recall (1990 film)">Total Recall</a></i> (1990), starring <a href="/wiki/Arnold_Schwarzenegger" title="Arnold Schwarzenegger">Arnold Schwarzenegger</a>, features <a href="/wiki/Taxicab" title="Taxicab">taxis</a> called <i>Johnny Cabs</i> controlled by artificial intelligence in the shape of an <a href="/wiki/Android_(robot)" title="Android (robot)">android</a> bust, while still possessing a joystick for manual control.</li>
<li>The film <i><a href="/wiki/Knight_Rider_2000" title="Knight Rider 2000">Knight Rider 2000</a></i> (1991) features a sentient and autonomous car called <a href="/wiki/KITT" title="KITT">KITT</a>.</li>
<li>The film <i><a href="/wiki/Jurassic_Park" title="Jurassic Park">Jurassic Park</a></i> (1993) has automatic tour vehicles which travel along a track. The <b style="color:#000;background:#ffcc99">cars</b> later become stuck after the power goes out and one of them get attacked by a T-Rex, who pushes it into a tree.</li>
<li>The film <i><a href="/wiki/Demolition_Man_(film)" title="Demolition Man (film)">Demolition Man</a></i> (1993), starring <a href="/wiki/Sylvester_Stallone" title="Sylvester Stallone">Sylvester Stallone</a> and set in 2032, features vehicles that can be <b style="color:#000;background:#ffff66">self</b>-driven or commanded to "Auto Mode" where a voice-controlled computer operates the vehicle.</li>
<li>The film <i><a href="/wiki/Timecop" title="Timecop">Timecop</a></i> (1994), starring <a href="/wiki/Jean-Claude_Van_Damme" title="Jean-Claude Van Damme">Jean-Claude Van Damme</a>, set in 2004 and 1994, has automated <b style="color:#000;background:#ffcc99">cars</b>.</li>
<li>The film <i><a href="/wiki/Inspector_Gadget_(film)" title="Inspector Gadget (film)">Inspector Gadget</a></i> (1999) features a <b style="color:#000;background:#ffff66">self</b>-<b style="color:#000;background:#66ffff">driving</b> car called the Gadgetmobile controlled by a comedic A.I. It also appears in the sequel <i><a href="/wiki/Inspector_Gadget_2" title="Inspector Gadget 2">Inspector Gadget 2</a></i> (2003).</li>
<li>Another Arnold Schwarzenegger movie, <i><a href="/wiki/The_6th_Day" title="The 6th Day">The 6th Day</a></i> (2000), features an automated car commanded by <a href="/wiki/Michael_Rapaport" title="Michael Rapaport">Michael Rapaport</a>.</li>
<li>The film <i><a href="/wiki/Minority_Report_(film)" title="Minority Report (film)">Minority Report</a></i> (2002), set in Washington, DC in 2054, features an extended chase sequence involving automated <b style="color:#000;background:#ffcc99">cars</b>. The vehicle of protagonist John Anderton is transporting him when its systems are overridden by police in an attempt to bring him into <a href="/wiki/Police_custody" class="mw-redirect" title="Police custody">custody</a>.</li>
<li>The film <i><a href="/wiki/Looney_Tunes:_Back_in_Action" title="Looney Tunes: Back in Action">Looney Tunes: Back in Action</a></i> (2003) features a spy car that can drive itself.</li>
<li>The film <i><a href="/wiki/The_Incredibles" title="The Incredibles">The Incredibles</a></i> (2004), <a href="/wiki/List_of_The_Incredibles_characters" title="List of The Incredibles characters">Mr. Incredible</a> makes his car (later revealed to be called the Incredibile) automated while it changes him into his supersuit when <b style="color:#000;background:#66ffff">driving</b> to catch up to a car of robbers on the run. The car reappears in the sequel <i><a href="/wiki/Incredibles_2" title="Incredibles 2">Incredibles 2</a></i> (2018) where it is used by Dash and Violet Parr to escape from brainwashed superheroes controlled by the villain Screenslaver and to board Winston Deavor's ship.</li></ul>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Audi_study.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Audi_study.JPG/220px-Audi_study.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Audi_study.JPG/330px-Audi_study.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Audi_study.JPG/440px-Audi_study.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="768" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Audi_study.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/I,_Robot_(film)" title="I, Robot (film)">I, Robot's</a></i> <a href="/wiki/Audi_RSQ" title="Audi RSQ">Audi RSQ</a> at the <a href="/wiki/CeBIT" title="CeBIT">CeBIT</a> expo in March 2005</div></div></div>
<ul><li>The film <i><a href="/wiki/I,_Robot_(film)" title="I, Robot (film)">I, Robot</a></i> (2004), set in <a href="/wiki/Chicago" title="Chicago">Chicago</a> in 2035, features automated vehicles <b style="color:#000;background:#66ffff">driving</b> on highways, allowing the car to travel safer at higher speeds than if manually controlled. The option to manually operate the vehicles is available.</li>
<li>In the film <i><a href="/wiki/Eagle_Eye_(2008_film)" class="mw-redirect" title="Eagle Eye (2008 film)">Eagle Eye</a></i> (2008) <a href="/wiki/Shia_LaBeouf" title="Shia LaBeouf">Shia LaBeouf</a> and <a href="/wiki/Michelle_Monaghan" title="Michelle Monaghan">Michelle Monaghan</a> are driven around in a <a href="/wiki/Porsche_Cayenne" title="Porsche Cayenne">Porsche Cayenne</a> that is controlled by ARIIA (a giant supercomputer).</li>
<li>In the film <i><a href="/wiki/Captain_America:_The_Winter_Soldier" title="Captain America: The Winter Soldier">Captain America: The Winter Soldier</a></i> (2014), <a href="/wiki/Nick_Fury_(Marvel_Cinematic_Universe)" title="Nick Fury (Marvel Cinematic Universe)">Nick Fury</a>'s SUV is capable of <b style="color:#000;background:#66ffff">driving</b> on its own.</li>
<li>The film <i><a href="/wiki/Hot_Tub_Time_Machine_2" title="Hot Tub Time Machine 2">Hot Tub Time Machine 2</a></i> (2015) features automated <b style="color:#000;background:#ffcc99">cars</b> that appear ten years in the future from the film's present time. One car targets Lou Dorchen after he insults it and it later helps the main characters return to the hot tub time machine after Lou apologizes to it for his insults.</li>
<li>In the CGI animated short film <i>You Are Not Alone</i> (2016), which is set in 2058, an automated car helps the main protagonist reach the surface to find her sister. The car later sacrifices itself to help the protagonist escape from the pursuing authorities.</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Geostorm" title="Geostorm">Geostorm</a></i> (2017), set in 2022, features a <b style="color:#000;background:#ffff66">self</b>-<b style="color:#000;background:#66ffff">driving</b> taxi stolen by protagonists Max Lawson and Sarah Wilson to protect the President from mercenaries and a superstorm.</li>
<li>The film <i><a href="/wiki/Logan_(film)" title="Logan (film)">Logan</a></i> (2017), set in 2029, features fully automated trucks.</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Blade_Runner_2049" title="Blade Runner 2049">Blade Runner 2049</a></i> (2017) opens with <a href="/wiki/LAPD" class="mw-redirect" title="LAPD">LAPD</a> <a href="/wiki/Replicant" title="Replicant">Replicant</a> cop K waking up in his modern <i>Spinner</i> (a <a href="/wiki/Flying_car" title="Flying car">flying police car</a>, now featuring automatic driver and separable surveillance roof drone) on approach to a protein farm in northern California.</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Upgrade_(film)" title="Upgrade (film)">Upgrade</a></i> (2018), set in a not too distant future, highlights the hazardous side to automated <b style="color:#000;background:#ffcc99">cars</b> as their <b style="color:#000;background:#66ffff">driving</b> systems can get hijacked and imperil the passengers.</li>
<li>In <i><a href="/wiki/Child%27s_Play_(2019_film)" title="Child&#39;s Play (2019 film)">Child's Play</a></i> (2019) <a href="/wiki/Chucky_(character)" title="Chucky (character)">Chucky</a> hijacks a <b style="color:#000;background:#ffff66">self</b>-<b style="color:#000;background:#66ffff">driving</b> "Kaslan Car" for the murder of Mike's mother, making it crash into normal <b style="color:#000;background:#ffcc99">cars</b> at a department store's parking lot.</li>
<li>In the film <i><a href="/wiki/Spies_in_Disguise" title="Spies in Disguise">Spies in Disguise</a></i> (2019), Lance Sterling's car is capable of <b style="color:#000;background:#66ffff">driving</b> autonomously.</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_literature">In literature</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Self-driving_car&amp;action=edit&amp;section=59" title="Edit section: In literature">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Intelligent or <b style="color:#000;background:#ffff66">self</b>-<b style="color:#000;background:#66ffff">driving</b> <b style="color:#000;background:#ffcc99">cars</b> are a common theme in <a href="/wiki/Science_fiction" title="Science fiction">science fiction</a> literature. Examples include:
</p>
<ul><li>In <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Asimov" title="Isaac Asimov">Isaac Asimov</a>'s science-fiction short story, "<a href="/wiki/Sally_(short_story)" title="Sally (short story)">Sally</a>" (first published May–June 1953), automated <b style="color:#000;background:#ffcc99">cars</b> have "<a href="/wiki/Positronic_brain" title="Positronic brain">positronic brains</a>" and communicate via honking horns and slamming doors, and save their human caretaker. Due to the high cost of the brain, few can afford a personal vehicle, so buses have become the norm.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Peter_F._Hamilton" title="Peter F. Hamilton">Peter F. Hamilton</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_Saga" title="Commonwealth Saga">Commonwealth Saga</a></i> series features intelligent or <b style="color:#000;background:#ffff66">self</b>-<b style="color:#000;background:#66ffff">driving</b> vehicles.</li>
<li>In <a href="/wiki/Robert_A_Heinlein" class="mw-redirect" title="Robert A Heinlein">Robert A Heinlein</a>'s novel, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Number_of_the_Beast_(novel)" title="The Number of the Beast (novel)">The Number of the Beast</a></i> (1980), Zeb Carter's <b style="color:#000;background:#66ffff">driving</b> and flying car "Gay Deceiver" is at first semi-automated and later, after modifications by Zeb's wife Deety, becomes sentient and capable of fully autonomous operation.</li>
<li>In <a href="/wiki/Edizioni_Piemme" title="Edizioni Piemme">Edizioni Piemme</a>'s series <i><a href="/wiki/Geronimo_Stilton" title="Geronimo Stilton">Geronimo Stilton</a></i>, a robotic vehicle called "Solar" is in the 54th book.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Alastair_Reynolds" title="Alastair Reynolds">Alastair Reynolds</a>' series, <i><a href="/wiki/Revelation_Space" title="Revelation Space">Revelation Space</a></i>, features intelligent or <b style="color:#000;background:#ffff66">self</b>-<b style="color:#000;background:#66ffff">driving</b> vehicles.</li>
<li>In <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Suarez_(author)" title="Daniel Suarez (author)">Daniel Suarez</a>' novels <i><a href="/wiki/Daemon_(novel_series)" title="Daemon (novel series)">Daemon</a></i> (2006) and <i><a href="/wiki/Freedom%E2%84%A2" title="Freedom™">Freedom™</a></i> (2010) driverless <b style="color:#000;background:#ffcc99">cars</b> and motorcycles are used for attacks in a software-based <a href="/wiki/Open-source_warfare" title="Open-source warfare">open-source warfare</a>. The vehicles are modified for this using <a href="/wiki/3D_printers" class="mw-redirect" title="3D printers">3D printers</a> and <a href="/wiki/Distributed_manufacturing" title="Distributed manufacturing">distributed manufacturing</a><sup id="cite_ref-317" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-317">&#91;317&#93;</a></sup> and are also able to operate as <a href="/wiki/Swarm_robotics" title="Swarm robotics">swarms</a>.</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_television">In television</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Self-driving_car&amp;action=edit&amp;section=60" title="Edit section: In television">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul><li>"Gone in 60 Seconds", season 2, episode 6 of 2015 TV series <i><a href="/wiki/CSI:_Cyber" title="CSI: Cyber">CSI: Cyber</a></i> features three seemingly normal customized vehicles, a 2009 <a href="/wiki/Nissan_Fairlady_Z" class="mw-redirect" title="Nissan Fairlady Z">Nissan Fairlady Z</a> Roadster, a <a href="/wiki/BMW_M3" title="BMW M3">BMW M3</a> E90 and a <a href="/wiki/Cadillac_CTS-V" class="mw-redirect" title="Cadillac CTS-V">Cadillac CTS-V</a>, and one stock luxury <a href="/wiki/BMW_7_Series" title="BMW 7 Series">BMW 7 Series</a>, being remote-controlled by a computer hacker.</li>
<li>"<a href="/wiki/Handicar" title="Handicar">Handicar</a>", season 18, episode 4 of 2014 TV series <i><a href="/wiki/South_Park" title="South Park">South Park</a></i> features a Japanese automated car that takes part in the <i><a href="/wiki/Wacky_Races_(1968_TV_series)" title="Wacky Races (1968 TV series)">Wacky Races</a></i>-style car race.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/KITT" title="KITT">KITT</a> and <a href="/wiki/KARR_(Knight_Rider)" title="KARR (Knight Rider)">KARR</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Pontiac_Trans_Am" class="mw-redirect" title="Pontiac Trans Am">Pontiac Firebird Trans-Ams</a> in the 1982 TV series <i><a href="/wiki/Knight_Rider_(1982_TV_series)" title="Knight Rider (1982 TV series)">Knight Rider</a></i>, were sentient and autonomous. The KITT and KARR based <a href="/wiki/Ford_Mustang_(fifth_generation)" title="Ford Mustang (fifth generation)">Ford Mustangs</a> from <a href="/wiki/Knight_Rider_(2008_TV_series)" title="Knight Rider (2008 TV series)">Knight Rider</a> were also sentient and autonomous, like their Firebird counterparts.</li>
<li>"Driven", series 4, episode 11 of the 2003 TV series <a href="/wiki/NCIS_(season_4)" title="NCIS (season 4)"><i>NCIS</i></a> features a robotic vehicle named "Otto", part of a high-level project of the Department of Defense, which causes the death of a Navy Lieutenant, and then later almost kills Abby.</li>
<li>The TV series <i><a href="/wiki/Viper_(TV_series)" title="Viper (TV series)">Viper</a></i> features a silver/grey armored assault vehicle, called <i>The Defender</i>, which masquerades as a flame-red 1992 <a href="/wiki/Dodge_Viper" title="Dodge Viper">Dodge Viper</a> RT/10 and later as a 1998 cobalt blue <a href="/wiki/Dodge_Viper_GTS" class="mw-redirect" title="Dodge Viper GTS">Dodge Viper GTS</a>. The vehicle's sophisticated computer systems allow it to be controlled via remote on some occasions.</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Black_Mirror" title="Black Mirror">Black Mirror</a></i> episode "<a href="/wiki/Hated_in_the_Nation" title="Hated in the Nation">Hated in the Nation</a>" briefly features a <b style="color:#000;background:#ffff66">self</b>-<b style="color:#000;background:#66ffff">driving</b> SUV with a touchscreen interface on the inside.</li>
<li><i>Bull</i> has a show discussing the effectiveness and safety of <b style="color:#000;background:#ffff66">self</b>-<b style="color:#000;background:#66ffff">driving</b> <b style="color:#000;background:#ffcc99">cars</b> in an episode call E.J.<sup id="cite_ref-318" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-318">&#91;318&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>"Rescue Bot Academy", season 3, episode 19 of <i><a href="/wiki/Transformers:_Rescue_Bots" title="Transformers: Rescue Bots">Transformers: Rescue Bots</a></i>, Chief Burns tells Jerry that the Autobot Blurr (whom Jerry had seen crash into a statue and discovered that there was no driver) is a <b style="color:#000;background:#ffff66">self</b>-<b style="color:#000;background:#66ffff">driving</b> car made by Doc Greene to prevent Blurr's secret from being revealed.</li>
<li>In <i><a href="/wiki/Mickey_Mouse_Mixed-Up_Adventures" title="Mickey Mouse Mixed-Up Adventures">Mickey Mouse Mixed-Up Adventures</a></i>, two <b style="color:#000;background:#ffff66">self</b>-<b style="color:#000;background:#66ffff">driving</b> vehicles are featured in the episodes <i>Mouse vs Machine</i> and <i>Super-Charged: Mickey's Monster Rally</i>: a hi-tech car called S.R.R. (<b style="color:#000;background:#ffff66">Self</b>-Racing Roadster) and a <b style="color:#000;background:#ffff66">self</b>-<b style="color:#000;background:#66ffff">driving</b> monster truck, which is actually Pete's Roadster, the <i>Super Crusher</i>, transformed by a ray gun called the Strengthenator.</li>
<li>In <i><a href="/wiki/SpongeBob_SquarePants" title="SpongeBob SquarePants">SpongeBob SquarePants</a></i>, a <b style="color:#000;background:#ffff66">self</b>-<b style="color:#000;background:#66ffff">driving</b> boatmobile named Coupe appears in the episode "Drive Happy".</li>
<li>In <i><a href="/wiki/Stroker_and_Hoop" class="mw-redirect" title="Stroker and Hoop">Stroker and Hoop</a></i>, a <b style="color:#000;background:#ffff66">self</b>-<b style="color:#000;background:#66ffff">driving</b> car named CARR (the acronym's meaning is unknown) appears throughout the series.</li>
<li>In <i><a href="/wiki/Lab_Rats_(American_TV_series)" title="Lab Rats (American TV series)">Lab Rats</a></i>, a <b style="color:#000;background:#ffff66">self</b>-<b style="color:#000;background:#66ffff">driving</b> car appears in the episode "Speed Trapped".</li>
<li>In <i><a href="/wiki/Team_Knight_Rider" title="Team Knight Rider">Team Knight Rider</a></i>, which is a spin-off of <i>Knight Rider</i>, seven autonomous vehicles appear in the series.</li>
<li>In <i><a href="/wiki/House_of_Mouse" title="House of Mouse">House of Mouse</a></i>, a <b style="color:#000;background:#ffff66">self</b>-<b style="color:#000;background:#66ffff">driving</b> car appears in the episode "Max's New Car" and in the <i><a href="/wiki/Mickey_Mouse_Works" title="Mickey Mouse Works">Mickey Mouse Works</a></i> cartoon "Mickey's New Car", which was featured in the episode itself.</li>
<li>In <i><a href="/wiki/Kim_Possible" title="Kim Possible">Kim Possible</a></i>, a <b style="color:#000;background:#ffff66">self</b>-<b style="color:#000;background:#66ffff">driving</b> car called SADI (Systemized Automotive <b style="color:#000;background:#66ffff">Driving</b> Intelligence) appears in the episode "Car Trouble".</li></ul>
